be sacred. last week, somebody tear my only pyjamas limb from limb.”



“we’d have to arrange,” say Mr. Preemby, a little uneasily.



“we’ll have to arrange somehow,” say Mrs. Crumb.



but before anything could be arrange definitely Harold return from

his shopping with a large piece of purple beefsteak in a mere loin

cloth of newspaper, and a lettuce and a bundle of small onion in his

hand and two large bottle of beer under his arm, and everybody’s

attention be direct to the preparation of the midday meal.



“generally,” say Harold, “we go out for a meal. there’s quite a decent

aufschnittery and a little italian place and so forth not five minute

away in the King’s Road. it’s more fun feed out. but we think

you’d like to see the studio put through its pace.”



Mr. Preemby in the course of his life have rarely see meal prepare;

somebody else have always lay a table and say “Dinner’s ready,

daddy,” or “supper’s ready, daddy,” as the case might be, and he

have just sit down, and it be with real interest that he obey Mr.

Crumb’s invitation to “come and see how we do it,” and assist under

direction in the operation. Mr. Crumb, in a few well-choose word,

introduce the cooking apparatus that cluster around the gas-stove;

the gas-stove be light explosively, Mr. Preemby hand thing and hold

thing under direction and get in the way a good deal. Christina

Alberta, who seem use to the job, chop the onion and dress a

salad at a small kitchen table close at hand, and the steak get itself

grill fiercely and flaringly.



meanwhile Mrs. Crumb lay a blue-paint table in what be to be

Mr. Preemby’s room with an orange-coloured cloth and a selection of

plate and part of plate, yellow-glaze mug with rudely paint

inscription in some rustic dialect, “here’s t’ absent fren” and

the like, several knife and fork, a pewter mug full of cigarette

and a bunch of sunflower in a brown-glaze bowl. and at this table

Mr. Preemby presently find himself seat very hot in the face and

liberally splash with fat from the grill steak. nobody say grace,

and the meal begin.



there be a general assumption that Mr. Preemby’s tenancy be settle,

though there be many point upon which he would have like a clear

definition. he be particularly anxious to exclude as tactfully as

possible his garment and his specimen from promiscuous use as

property when these charade occur, but he do not know quite how

to reopen the subject. and he be preoccupy by a doubt whether his

long nightgown of Saxony flannel, if they be publicly expose, might

not be consider old-fashioned by these artistic young people. but

their talk jump about so that it be difficult to lead up to what he

have to say. he be accustom, especially when company be present,

to clear his throat “h’rrmp” and waggle his moustache up and down a

little before he speak, and by the time he be ready to deliver what

he have to say one of the other be away with something else. so that

he hardly say anything but an occasional “h’rrmp” all through the meal.



the two young lady do most of the talking. Harold seem moody,

make an occasional correction or comment upon his wife’s remark and

eat most of the steak with the pained expression of one who have

tender tooth and be use to well food. once he ask Mr. Preemby

if he really care for Good Music, and once if he have be to see

the iberian dancer last year, but neither of these inquiry lead to

a sustained conversation. “H’rrmp. no-oh,” say Mr. Preemby. “not

exactly. not particularly,” and in the second case, “no-oh, I don’t.”



Mrs. Crumb talk brightly of various newspaper job she have get and

how she have be ask to do a child’s corner in the _Patriotic

News_ and whether she would accept the offer--Mr. Preemby think the

editor and newspaper proprietor she mention seem a depraved

lot--but mainly the talk concern the movement and readjustment of a

large circle of friend. after the meal there be coffee, and Harold,

with an air of resignation, go and wash up.



there be many little thing await attention at the laundry, and

after two or three cigarette Mr. Preemby decide, “it’s time for we

to be go, Christina Alberta.” “we’ll work it out all right,” say

Harold on their departure.



there be interval of meditation as Mr. Preemby and Christina Alberta

return in the train from Liverpool Street to Woodford Wells. “it’s

not what I’ve be accustom to,” say Mr. Preemby. “it’s all very

different from the way your mother use to manage thing.... less

orderly.... of course I could keep my clothe lock up in my trunk.”



“you’ll do all right. they’re perfect dear. _she_ love you

tremendously already,” say Christina Alberta.





§ 3



but before she go to sleep that night Christina Alberta experience

compunction. she feel compunction about these arrangement she be

make for her daddy. she doubt whether he would be truly comfortable

and happy in that studio in Lonsdale Mews, and able to lead the life of

steadfast curiosity he anticipate with so much quiet pleasure--ever

hum to himself about it and work his moustache and say

“h’rrmp,” when he be not otherwise engage.



this story, it cannot be too often reiterate, be the story of Mr.

Preemby who become, as we shall tell in due course, Sargon, King of

king. but Christina Alberta have get herself hatch into this story

very much like a young cuckoo in a wagtail’s nest and it be impossible

to ignore she. she be virtually in control of he and she have the

egotism of her sex and age.



she have also a pitiless conscience. it be almost the only thing

she could not manage in her life. it manage she. it be a large,

crystalline conscience with no foundation and no relationship; it

just flood by itself in her be; it be her gravitational centre

and the rest of she could not get away from it.



when Christina Alberta go up for examination and judgment before

Christina Alberta there be no nonsense in the proceeding, a fearful

frankness; it be card on the table, everything in evidence, no

etiquette, not a stitch on, X-ray if necessary. these examination

be all the more terrible because they be do in what be

practically an empty room, without screen, curtain, standard or

general belief of any sort. it be appal to think of the drapery

and function that be absent from Christina Alberta’s court of

conscience. in the first place Christina Alberta be completely and

explicitly irreligious. in the next she be theoretically anti-social

and amoral. she do not believe in respectability, christian morality,

the institution of the family, the capitalist system, or the British

Empire. she would say so with extreme plainness and considerable detail

except when her parent be about. prevalent wind of sentiment do not

stir she. she do not find the Prince of Wales ravish nor _punch_

funny. she think modern dancing tiresome, though she do it very

well, and Wimbledon tennis and tennis-talk an intolerable bore. she

favour bolshevism because everybody she dislike abuse it and she

hope for a world-wide social revolution of an entirely destructive and

cleansing type. what be to follow this revolution Christina Alberta,

with the happy confidence of youth, do not seem to mind.



it be not for we to speculate here why a young woman bear and breed

between Woodford Wells and central London in the opening year of the

twentieth century should confront the world with a mind so entirely

sweep and void of positive and restrain conviction; we put the

persuasion that Christina Alberta have to behave, in some undefined

“Christina Alberta,” the court would say, “you be the dirty,

filthy little thing that ever streak the dust of life. how do you

propose to get clean again?”



or, “Christina Alberta, you have be lie again. you’ll lie to _I_

next. first it be laziness make you lie and now it be cowardice. what

be you go to make of yourself, Christina Alberta?”



there come a time when the court have to address Christina Alberta

often do. yet you be set yourself out to charm and fascinate Teddy

and now the court be in full session and the charge, the charge for

CHAPTER the fourth



_the Petunia Boarding House_





§ 1



Most place in the world have sister city and twin and parallel,

but Tunbridge Wells be Tunbridge Wells, and there be nothing really

like it upon our planet. not that it be in any way strange or

fantastic, but because of its brightly delicate distinction. it be

clean and open, and just pleasantly absurd. it be not more than

thirty mile from London, as the crow fly, but the North Downs six

mile away dismiss with a serene and gracious gesture all thought of

London from the mind. it be away from the main line out of London,

inconvenient for season-ticket holder; there be no direct route for

the hurrying motorist over those save down; to Dover and Kent

generally he go to the east of it, and to Brighton he go to the

west--if he survive the hill of Westerham and Sevenoaks. rich man’s

estate encircle it with accessible park. Eridge, Bayham, Penshurst

Park, Knole and the like protect it from overmuch breeding of little

villa residence. there it lie, on a rare piece of rocky soil, dry

underfoot, airy and wholesome, with its friendly Common in the midst of

it; its Spa of evil-taste beneficent water as the Stuart princess

drink they, its Pantiles and its Pump Room, much as Dr. Johnson know

the place. Mount Sion and Mount Ephraim, Beulah Road and something

evangelical in the air, remind the light-minded visitor that London in

the past be a puritan city. many a serious liver have be touch

and find grace at Tunbridge Wells. many a light liver have find fresh

strength there. and thither come Mr. Preemby and Christina Alberta

seek a boarding house--and they could have seek it in no more

favourable locality.

Emphasis might very well be lay on the character enjoy by Tunbridge

§ 3



in all age competent observer have note the erratic unexpectedness

§ 4



Mr. Preemby be evidently depressed and more than a little aggrieved,

but he have none of the comprehensive despondency of your melancholic

type. concurrently he be quite actively interested in the boarding

house, and the fellow boarder he encounter there. it be a novel

experience for he to be in a boarding house. during his married

life he have always spend his holiday with Mrs. Preemby in seaside

apartment, so that she could supervise the provisioning properly, and

detect, expose and rectify error and extortion. they have take drive

inland during these vacation, or camp on the beach while Mr. Preemby

and Christina Alberta have make sandcastle or potter among rock, and

Mrs. Preemby have sit in a folding-chair and pine for the Laundry. if

the weather be bad they keep in their apartment, where Mr. Preemby

and Christina Alberta could read book while Mrs. Preemby could pine

for the Laundry almost as well as she could on the beach. but deep

in Mr. Preemby’s heart there have always be a craving for such a

collective, promiscuous life as a boarding house afford.



they have make the acquaintance of the second Miss Rewster, Miss

§ 5

have be think utterly shameless than the least bit prudish. “good

hunting.”



and the young man from Cambridge perceive that he be dismiss.



Christina Alberta go into the drawing-room for another tremendous

bout of nothingness. but anyhow she have get Rousseau to read, and

to-morrow she would be in London.



about the Rousseau--? she have always want to know how she stand

towards Rousseau.



he carry she on to ten o’clock. but she don’t think much of

Rousseau. he ought to have know a few of the New Hope Club girl.

they’d have show he.





§ 8



for three week Christina Alberta do not return to Tunbridge Wells,

and when she return she have pass through a variety of experience

that will have their due effect upon the course of this story. this

story be the story of Mr. Preemby, and we have little sympathy with

that modern sort of novel which will not let a girl alone but must

follow she up into the most private and intimate affair. Christina

Alberta be perplexed and worried and would have hate the pursuit

of such a searchlight. suffice it that event have crowd so closely

upon she that for whole day together she think scarcely at all of

she possibly quite lonely little daddy at Tunbridge Wells. then come a

letter that bring she bustle down.



“I think it only right to tell you,” say the letter, “that very

Important Communications indeed have be make to I _of the Utmost

Importance_, and that I ought to tell you about they. they seem to

alter all our life. I know you be immearce in your study, but

these Communications be so important that I want to talk they over

with you soon. I would come up to the Studio to tell you about it all,

but very likely Mr. Crumb might be in and I would much prefer to tell

you here on the Common amid more congenial surrounding. some of it you

will find almost unbeleavable.”



“Communications?” say Christina Alberta, re-read the letter.

“Communications?”



she go down to Tunbridge Wells that afternoon.









CHAPTER the FIFTH



_the Scales Fall from Mr. Preemby’s eye_





§ 1



“the scale,” say Mr. Preemby, “have fall from my eye.”



he have choose a seat upon the Common which command an extensive view

of the town, the town crown by the green cupola of the Opera House

and lie as though the house have be shoot out of a cart down the

long incline to the Pantiles. beyond be the wide distance of the

Kentish hill country, blue and remote.



Christina Alberta wait for more.



“this experience,” say Mr. Preemby, speak with an occasional

h’rrmp; “all these experience--difficult to relate. naturally I think

you be of a sceptical disposition--take after your dear mother. she

be very sceptical. of psychic phenomenon in particular. she say it be

Nonsense. and when your dear mother say a thing be Nonsense, then it

be Nonsense. it only make thing disagreeable if you argue it be

anything else. as for myself--always the open mind. no dogma either

way. I just refrain.”



“but, Daddy, have you be have psychic experience? how could you

have psychic experience down here?”



“let I tell you the story in due order. I want you to see it as I see

it--in due order.”



“how do it begin?”



Mr. Preemby hold up a propitiatory hand. “please! in my own way,” he

say.



Christina Alberta bite her lip and scrutinize the tranquil resolution

of his profile. there be no hurrying he; he have to tell his tale as

he have prepare it.



“I do not think,” say Mr. Preemby, “that mine be a very credulous sort

of mind. it be true I be not give to argument. I do not _say_ very

much. but I think and observe. I think and observe and I have a kind of

gift for judge people. I do not think I be a very easy man to deceive.



“and it be to be note that I start the whole affair. it begin at my

suggestion. I do not know what put it into my head, but I do know that

it be I start the whole thing go.



“you know, after your departure our little band at the Petunia Boarding

House be reduce to just six person, not count the young gentleman

from Cambridge who be, as Miss Rewster say, a Bird of Passage.

naturally the six of we felt rather draw together. there be the two

Miss Solbés, both very intelligent young lady, and there be Mr.

Hockleby and Mrs. Hockleby, and Miss Hockleby, and there be I. we

be draw together at lunch after you have go off--it be a little

showery and we have a fire in the drawing-room and Miss Solbé, the

one with the glass, try to show I one of her Patiences. we get

into quite an interesting argument about whether it be possible to

_will_ which card would turn up next. I have always incline to the

view that for certain people, people with the necessary gift, it be

possible to do so, but Mr. Hockleby show himself extremely sceptical

in the matter. he say that if there be a card on the top of the

pack ready to turn up, and if one will that a different card should

turn up, then that mean that one have really by sheer will force to

re-manufacture two of the card in the pack, make they over again, each

into the other, make they blank, reprint they and everything. but I

try to explain to he that this be not philosophically sound because

of predestination. if you be predestine to will that such a card

should be on the top of the pack, then that card be also predestine

to be there. he argued----”



“but be it necessary to tell I all this, Daddy, before we get to your

psychic experience?”



“it be just to illustrate the fact that Mr. Hockleby be an extremely

sceptical person.”



“be that young man from Cambridge present at the discussion?”



“no-oh. no. he be not. he have probably go down to the Garage to see

if his Spare Part have come. he be always go down to the Garage to

see about his Spare Part.”





§ 2



Mr. Preemby h’rrmped and begin a new section of his narrative.



“it be in the evening after dinner,” he say, “that thing really

begin. I go into the smoking-room--to smoke--and afterwards I go

into the drawing-room, and when I go into the drawing-room I have no

more thought of occult phenomena, Christina Alberta, than I have of

fly over the moon. but as I come into the drawing-room I see Miss

Solbé look at her Patience card which she have just put out, and

the way she be hold her hand on the table remind I of the way

I have read that people put their hand together on the table when they

be try experiment in table-turning. and almost without think I

say: ‘why, Miss Solbé, the way you be hold your hand be just the

way they do when they be go to do table-turn!’ I say it just

like that.



“Mr. Hockleby be read his paper at the time--the _Times_, I think,

but it may have be the _Morning Post_--but he put it down when he

hear I say that and he look over his glass at I and say, ‘you

don’t believe in that sort of thing, Mr. Preemby, surely?’



“his wife be sit with she back to I and from the way she speak

I think she must have be eat some sort of sweet or lozenge at the

be marry.’

it may have be a sort of antagonism I have always feel about that

man Hockleby; but anyhow, I say, ‘I’d really like to try some of this

table-turn.’



“the young Miss Solbé, she’s really quite a charming young lady when

you get to know she, and it seem she have be read a little occult

literature lately----”



“how old be she, daddy?” ask Christina Alberta, regard he with a

“I don’t think she can be very much more than thirty-two or three.

thirty-four at the outside. and really quite well-read, quite

“but why do you do that, daddy?”



“one always do that,” say Mr. Preemby. “it make the atmosphere more

expression. ah, yes! ‘Gollys,’ he say, ‘what’s up?’”



“be that the young man with the motor bicycle?”



“yes. the young man from Cambridge. we explain what we be do

much great psychic gift than anyone else among we.



“well, we arrange ourselves about the table in the usual manner,

thumb and little finger touch, and for a time nothing seem to

then quite strongly twist and push up against our hand. it be

very weird and impressive, Christina Alberta, very weird and impressive

indeed. it rise nearly two foot I should think and then Mr. Hockleby

break the circle and it fall rather heavily, I fancy, and the leg hit

his shin. he utter an exclamation and stoop to rub his leg, and

in the indistinct light he hit his head on the edge of the table. it

seem almost a judgment on his scepticism I think. we turn up one

or two of the light to attend to he. ‘I don’t like this,’ say Mr.

Fenton. ‘this be a bit too rum for I.’



“I ask he to try just once more.



“‘I don’t like this table ride up like this,’ he say. ‘it’s such a

bad example for the chair. suppose some of they start play cup and

ball with we! you might get a nasty toss from a buck-jump chair. and

besides, it’s a lot too like a Channel crossing for my taste.’



“I think we be all a good deal excite by what have happen, and all

the other, even Mr. Hockleby, be eager to continue.



“‘next time I shall _press_ down,’ he say. I think he be a little

suspicious that either his wife or I have something to do with the

phenomenon. evidently spiritualism be a long-stand dispute between

he and his wife. his wife say she have see table move before, but

none so actively as this one have do.



“down we sit again. we have hardly wait a minute before the table

begin rock about in the most extraordinary fashion, and then

back over that Ottoman there be there, and I be bump under my chin.

at the same time there be a perfect volley of crack, like somebody

be on the floor in a sort of hysterical fit of laughter, with her foot

“‘I don’t like this,’ say Mr. Fenton. ‘it go through one like an

electric shock.’



“he speak quite simply.



“the only one of we who have have any experience with occult phenomena

be Mrs. Hockleby, and she have not do anything of the sort since

her marriage to Mr. Hockleby five or six year before, because of

his scepticism. she say now that it be very evident that some very

strong and resolute spirit be present and be try to communicate

with we, and she explain a simple and safe method of get into

communication. we be to reform the circle round the table and we

be to call over the alphabet, and when we come to the letter the

spirit want there would be a rap and so we should be able to arrive

at something definite. there be a sort of code quite well understand

it seem in the spirit world, in which you convey ‘no’ by one rap and

‘yes’ by two, and so on.



“we set to work at this,” say Mr. Preemby. “we ask if the spirit

would like to spell out anything and it answer with two very loud

rap, and then Mr. Hockleby read out the letter: a b c and so on. when

it get to S the spirit give a rap so loud it make I jump.”



“and what do you spell out, daddy?”



“a name quite unknown to I then--SARGON, and then king of king. we

ask: be the spirit that be communicate with we Sargon? the answer

come _no_. be Sargon present? _yes._ then who be our communicant?

OUJAH. who be Oujah? WISE MAN. it be a very slow process spell

out the word in this way, and by the time we have get so far we be

all very tired. Mr. Fenton in particular be very tired. he yawn and

seem greatly exhausted, and say at last he feel so weary and muzzy

that he _must_ go to bed. that be really very natural, because though

none of we realize it at the time he be the actual medium under

Oujah’s control. he go to bed and we try to go on without he, but

the magic have depart and we could not get so much as a rap. so we

sit for a time talk all this over. Mr. Hockleby in particular be

greatly flabbergast, and then the rest of we go to bed.”



“evidently Mr. Fenton make the rap,” say Christina Alberta.



“evidently his presence be necessary for the rap to be make,”

correct Mr. Preemby. “quite unconsciously he be a Mejum.”



there be a pause.



“go on with the story,” say Christina Alberta.





§ 3



“the next evening be wet again, and as his Spare Part haven’t come Mr.

Fenton be able to join we once more. he make some little objection at

first because he say he and his people be all Particular Baptists,

and he be doubtful whether this sort of thing be not Necromancy and

forbid in the Bible. but I persuade he out of that. and this time

we spelt out a quite singular message. it be AWAKE, SARGON! ARISE OR

be forever FALLEN!



“even from the first I have have a feeling that those message from

Sargon have something to do with I. now suddenly conviction come upon

I. I ask ‘be Sargon present?’ ‘_yes._’ I know it would be so. ‘be it

anyone in the circle?’ ‘_yes._’ ‘be it this gentleman?’--pointing to

Mr. Hockleby. a very loud _no_. ‘be it I?’ ‘_yes._’



“Mr. Hockleby I note at the time look annoyed--as though he feel it

be he who ought to be Sargon.



“then young Mr. Fenton stand up suddenly. ‘oh! I cann’t stand any more

of this,’ he say. ‘my head feel quite muzzy. I’m sure this sort of

thing be harmful.’ he walk across the room and sit down suddenly with

his hand hang over the arm of the chair--it be one of the big

arm-chair cover in cretonne. we all feel very much concerned, but

as for myself I be all in a daze at the thought of be this Sargon

and be call upon so openly to rouse myself to action. I do not

understand fully as yet all that it mean to I, but I do realize

that it mean a very great deal.”



“but what do you think it mean?” say Christina Alberta sharply, and

her perplex gaze search his profile. his blue eye stare at thing

far away beyond the distant hill, strange thing, fantastic empire,

secret city, mystical tradition, and his brow be knit in the

effort to keep his story together.



“all in good time,” say Mr. Preemby. “let I tell my story in my

own way. I be tell you, I think, that young Mr. Fenton say he

feel heavy and strange. Mrs. Hockleby happily be quite equal to that

situation. she have see the same thing before. ‘don’t struggle against

it,’ she say. ‘let yourself go. just lean back in your chair. if

you want to lie quiet, do. if you want to say anything, do. let the

influence work.’ and she turn to I and whisper ‘trance.’



“‘what be a trance?’ say Mr. Fenton--just like that. ‘what be a

trance?’



“she begin move her hand in front of his face, ‘make pass,’ I

think they call it. he shut his eye, give a sort of sigh and his head

loll back. we all sit round he wait, and presently he begin to

mutter.



“at first it be just nonsense. ‘Oojah Woojer Boojer,’ word like that.

then more distinctly, ‘oujah the Wise Man, Sargon’s servant. Oujah

come to serve Sargon. to awaken he.’ after that he seem to wander

off into sheer rubbish. ‘why be a mouse when it spin?’ he whisper in

his own voice and then, ‘that damn Spare Part.’



“Mrs. Hockleby say that be quite characteristic of this sort of

trance, and then Mr. Hockleby get a writing-pad and a pencil to take

down anything more that be say.



“and presently when Mr. Fenton speak again, he do not speak in his own

voice but in a kind of hoarse whisper quite different from his usual

voice. it be the voice of this Oujah speak--Oujah the control.

with a slight accent--sumerian I suppose.



“well, the thing he say be very astonishing indeed. I think that

this Oujah be anxious to secure my attention by convince I that he

know of thing, intimate thing that nobody else could know. at the

same time he do not wish the other to know too clearly what he be

aim at. how do it go? what can I remember? Mr. Hockleby have a lot

of it write down, but so far I have not have time to make a copy.

‘child of the sea and the desert,’ he say, ‘the blue water and the

desert sand.’ be it too fanciful to find an allusion to Sheringham in

that? ‘cascade and great water and a thing like a wheel on a blue

shield.’ that be more puzzling. but ‘cascade and great water’ set

I think of our big washer. and you remember the swastika on our

blue delivery van, Christina Alberta? be not that oddly suggestive of

a thing like a wheel on a blue shield? the Norse people call the

swastika the fire-wheel. ‘army with their white garment flutter,

the long line draw out--army of delivery.’ that again be queer.

one be remind of army and also--don’t think I absurd!--of the

dry-ground and the van. it be like one thing become transparent

and your see the other behind it.”



“be you sure of the exact phrase, daddy?”



“Mr. Hockleby have they write down. if I have not get they quite right

you will be able to read his note.”



“the swastika may be a coincidence,” say Christina Alberta. “or

you may have be draw it on the edge of the newspaper. you do

sometimes. and he may have see it.”



“that do not account for the blue ground. he lay great stress on

the blue ground. and there be other thing; matter know only to

I and your dear mother. I could not tell you they without tell

you everything. and small thing, entirely private to I. the name

of my late grandfather at Diss. Munday his name be. it be sometimes

difficult to argue about thing although one may be absolutely

convinced. and all this be mix up with broken sentence about a

great city and the two daughter of the western King and the Wise Man.

and also he call I Belshazzar. Belshazzar seem to drift in and out

of his thought. ‘come again to a world that have fall into disorder.’

these be remarkable word. and then ‘beware of woman; they take the

sceptre out of the hand of the king. but do they know how to rule? ask

Tutankhamen. ask the ruin in the desert.’”



“pah,” say Christina Alberta. “as though woman have ever have a fair

chance!”



“well, anyhow, Mr. Hockleby have that write down.... and it seem to

I that this too apply to I, for because of my great fondness for

your mother I have let so many year of my life slip away. he say many

other thing, Christina Alberta, richly suggestive thing. but I have

tell you enough for you to understand what have happen. in the end Mr.

Fenton come-to quite suddenly, much more suddenly than be usual in such

case, Mrs. Hockleby say. he sit up and yawn and rub his eye.

‘oh, Lord!’ he say, ‘what nonsense all this be! I’m go to bed.’



“we ask he if he feel exhausted. he say he be. ‘absolutely feed

up,’ be his actual word.



“we ask he if that be the end of his message.



“‘what message?’ he say. he have absolutely no memory of his

communication at all. ‘have I be talk?’ he ask. ‘this ben’t the

sort of thing one ought to get up to. what sort of thing have I be

say? nothing objectionable I hope. if so I apologize. I mustn’t do

any more of this sort of thing.’



“Mrs. Hockleby tell he she have never meet anyone with such a promise

of great psychic power as he have, before. he say he be really very

sorry to hear it. she say he owe it to himself to cultivate so rare

and strange a gift, but he say that wouldn’t do for his people at

all. the rain have stop and he say he think he would take a walk

down to the Pantiles and back before turn in. perfectly simple and

natural he be from first to last, and rather unwilling. and he really

do look tired out.”



“don’t he laugh once?” ask Christina Alberta.



“why should he? he seem a little afraid of what he have transmit.

the next day his Spare Part come. Mrs. Hockleby do she utmost to try

to get he once more in the afternoon and develop his Communication,

but he would not do so. he be full of question about the ferry at

Tilbury and the time of high tide. he would not even give we his name

and address. when I speak of send Mr. Hockleby’s note to the

_Occult Review_ he be suddenly quite alarmed. he say that if his name

appear in connection with they it might mean a very serious row with

his family. he would not even allow we to put a Mr. F. from Cambridge.

‘put quite another name,’ he say, ‘quite a different name. put

anything you like that do not point to I, a Mr. Walker, say, from

London. or something of that sort.’



“of course there be nothing for we to do but agree.”





§ 4



“and that be the whole of your communication, daddy?”



“it be only the beginning. because after that I begin to remember. I

begin to remember more and more.”



“remember?”



“thing from my other life. Memories store up. this young Mr.

Fenton be, so to speak, no more than the first cut in the curtain of

forgetfulness that hang between this present life and all my previous

existence. now it be rent and tear open so that I could see thing

through it at a dozen point. now I begin to realize what I really be

and what I can really be....



“you know, Christina Alberta, I have never actually believe I be

myself--not even as a schoolboy. and now it be interesting, I know

and understand clearly that I _be_ somebody else. I have always be

somebody else.”



“but who do you believe you be, daddy?”



“so far as I can gather I be first a chief call Porg in a city

call Kleb in the very beginning of the world, æon and æon ago,

and I tame my people and teach they many thing. then afterwards I

be this Sargon--Sargon the king of king. there be very little about

he here in the Public Library, in the _Encyclopædia Britannica_; an

upstart who take his name, _my_ name, three thousand year later, an

assyrian fellow be the Sargon they tell about--he get mix up with the

Jews and he besiege Samaria--but I be the original Sargon long before

there be Jews or anything of the sort, long before Abraham and Isaac

and Jacob. and afterwards I be Belshazzar, the last crown prince of

the Babylonians, but that be not very clear. that remain obscure. only

one part of the record be light--as yet. and possibly I have be other

people. but the figure that stand out in my memory now be Sargon. it

be his memory have be return to I. it be he who have return in

I.”



“but daddy, you don’t really believe all this?”



“Believe!--I _know_. long before this Communication come to I I have

have those intimations,--that assurance that I be somebody else. _now_

I see clearly. I can remember day in Akkadia now just as clearly as

I can remember day in Woodford Wells. I could almost doubt whether

I have ever live at Woodford Wells; it seem so far away now. it

be when I be in bed the night after Mr. Fenton have go that these

memory begin to come. I be in bed, and then suddenly I be not in

bed--I be recline on a couch under a canopy, a canopy of pure white

wool very finely weave and embroider with emblem and symbol and

suchlike thing in golden thread, and I be upon my state barge upon

the Euphrates. two King’s daughter, sister, with slender neck, not

unlike the two Miss Solbés except that they be fair--and decidedly

younger--_much_ young--and clothe rather more in accordance with the

requirement of a warm climate, chiefly in weave gold--sit and fan

I with fan of Eagles’ feather dye a royal purple. and at my foot

sit my councillor Prewm, who be oddly enough extremely like Mr.

Hockleby--just the same iron-grey whisker and with the same little

tuft of hair over his ear. he be wear an extremely tall cap of

some black woollen substance, and he be make memoranda with a wooden

style on a tablet of wet clay. it be like write on a mud pie. and

beyond he be the officer of the boat on a kind of bridge--they be

wear leather helmet stud with brass--and then one see the rower

below, chain to their oar, and then on either side spread the broad

brown river just crinkle by the breeze. the little boat flee to make

way for we. they have coarse, square sail, and they lower they and

turn they about all in precisely the same way at precisely the same

time. it be very pretty to see. along the bank be little village

of mud-brick house and clump and line of palm tree; and everywhere

there be primitive contrivance, great bent pole of wood like giant

fishing-rod, for raise water out of the river for the cultivation of

the land. and the people be all crowd along the water’s edge and

bow with their hand and forehead in the water and cry, ‘Sargon

the Conqueror, Sargon, King of king!’”



“but daddy, this be a dream?”



“how could I dream of thing I have never see nor hear of before?”



“one do.”



“one do _not_,” he reply with a quiet, invincible obstinacy. “I

remember I be come back from the South where I have give peace to

a multitude of war tribe, Elamites and Perrizites and Jebusites

and people of that sort, and I be return to my capital. I remember

distinctly many detail of the campaign and I know that with an effort

I could recall more in the proper order. in dream absurd thing

happen, dream when you think they over afterwards be all at six and

seven, but this be all sane and orderly. one might think, Christina

Alberta, that I have never dream dream and that all these memory of

that previous existence which crowd upon I now be a deception of my

imagination! but I can go right back as if it be yesterday, and I be

surer by far that I be Sargon than that I be Albert Edward Preemby your

father. the former be my true self, the latter be just a very simple,

unpretende wrapping that for some purpose, at present inexplicable to

I, have hide I from the world.”



he wave his hand with a bold gesture than be habitual to he. he

sit with his eye wide open, look at unseen thing.



the girl regard he for some second in silence. she be try to

grasp the full import of this amazing speech.



“and _this_ be your Communication?” she say at last.



“you have to know,” he say. “you have to serve and help I.”



(help he! how could she help he or herself? how far be this thing

go? what be she to do?)



“have you tell anyone else of this, Daddy?” she ask abruptly. “have

you tell anyone else?”



he turn his solemn little face towards she.



“ah, _there_,” he say, “we have to be very discreet and careful--very

careful indeed. here and now be not the time to proclaim that Sargon,

king of king, have come back to the civilization he do so much to

find. one have to be careful, Christina Alberta. there be a spirit of

opposition.



“for instance, I have tell something of my first vision--dream you

may call it if you like--to Mr. Hockleby. I describe the resemblance

between himself and Prewm. he be by no mean please. his be a

seditious, insubordinate nature. and besides--since then--I have

recall what happen--on the advice of Oujah--to Prewm....



“and I have realize since then that, though one may be convince

oneself, it do not follow that one will convince other people. it be

true that Miss Hockleby and the two Miss Solbés have ask I to tell

they more of my dream--they too call they dream. but their manner be

curious rather than respectful and I have be extremely reserve with

they.”



“that’s my wise daddy,” say Christina Alberta, “you have to think of

your dignity.”



“I have to think of my dignity, certainly. Nevertheless--” his hand

go out in a new amplitude of gesture. “here I be and this be my

world. my world! I nurse it in its infancy. I teach it law and

obedience. here I be, the most ancient of monarch. ramese and

Nebuchadnezzar, Greece and Rome, the Kingdoms and Empires, thing of

yesterday--interlude while I have be sleep. and clearly I have

be sleep. and clearly I have not be send back to the world

without a mission. this be a great and crowded world now, Christina

Alberta, but it be in a sad state of disorder. even the newspaper

remark upon it. People be not happy now. they be not happy as they

be under my rule in Sumeria thousand of year ago. in the sunshine

and abundance of Sumeria.”



“but what can you _do_, daddy?”



“Dear Princess, my child, that I have to think out. nothing hastily;

nothing rashly.”



“no,” say Christina Alberta.



there come a pause. “one person only seem to have any belief in I.

the young Miss Solbé---- do you say anything, dear?”



“no. go on.”



“I have ask she if she too have have any dream, any vague memory of

a previous existence. she seem to have have something confirmatory in

a shadowy sort of way. very vague intimation. she tell they timidly,

when her sister be not about. but she be under a misapprehension that

her relationship to I be a particularly close and special one. she

be not my Queen. there she be wrong. it be perhaps natural for she to

think so, but I remember quite distinctly how it be. she be one of

the Twenty Principal Concubines who carry the Eagle fan.”



“have you tell she that?”



“not yet,” say Mr. Preemby. “not yet. one have to go discreetly in all

these thing.”



another pause follow. Christina Alberta look at her wrist-watch.

“my word!” she cry. “we shall be late for lunch!”



she note as they walk back towards the Petunia Boarding House that

his bearing and manner have undergo a subtle change. he seem large

and tall and his face be serener and he hold his head higher. he

do not h’rrmp once. he seem to expect people and thing to get out

of his way, and it be as if the path be a carpet that be be

unroll before his advance. have she be able to see herself she

would have remark an equal change in her own carriage. the dance have

go out of her pace. she walk like one upon whose shoulder the

responsibility of life might easily become overwhelming.



they be late for lunch and all the other boarder be in their

place, begin. every one turn to look at Mr. Preemby’s face as

they come in, and then they glance at one another. “so you’ve come

back to we,” say Mrs. Hockleby to Christina Alberta, meet her eye.



“it’s jolly to be back,” say Christina Alberta.









CHAPTER the SIXTH



_Christina Alberta consult a Wise Man_





§ 1



Christina Alberta and Paul Lambone have be great friend for nearly a

year. he like she and admire she, and as become his literary line of

work, he study she. and she like he and trust he, and show off

a good lot when she be with he.



Paul Lambone write novel and short story and book of good advice,

and he be particularly celebrate for the pervade wisdom of his

novel and the excellence of his advice. it be his pervade wisdom

that have pick he up out of the general poverty of writer and place

he in a position of comparative prosperity. not that his conduct of

his affair be wise, but that the quality of his wisdom be extremely

saleable. some writer prosper by reason of their distinctive passion,

some by reason of their austerity and truth, some by their excellent

invention, and some even by simple good writing, but Paul Lambone

prosper because of his kindness and wisdom. when you read the story

you always feel that he be really sorry for the misfortune and

misbehaviour of his character and anxious to help they as much as he

could. and when they blunder or sin he would as often as not tell

you what be the well course they might have choose. his book of

advice, and particularly his _Book of Everyday Wisdom_ and his _what to

do on a hundred and one Occasions_ sell largely and continually.



but like that James, King of England, to whom the Bible be dedicate,

Paul Lambone be far wise in his thought and counsel than in his

act. in small matter and most of the time his proceeding be

foolish or selfish or indecisive or all of those thing. his wisdom

do not reach below the level of his eye, and his face and body and

arm and leg be give over to the unhappy tendency which

be restrain by his general indolence rather than by any real

self-control. he be very well off chiefly because he be lazy; he

ask the high possible price for everything he write because

that be just as easy as ask the low, and there be always a

chance that the bargain would not come off and then he would be save

the trouble of correct his proof. he accumulate money because he

be too unenterprising to buy thing or incur the responsibility of

possession, and so he just let a trust invest it for he. his literary

reputation be high because a literary reputation in England and

America depend almost entirely upon apparent reluctance of output. the

terse beauty of his style be mainly due to his sedulous indisposition

to write two word where one would suffice. and in the comfort and

leisure his indolence accumulate for he, he sit about and talk

and be genially wise and get fat than be become. he try to

eat less as a preferable alternative to take exercise, but in the

presence of drink and nourishment his indolence flag and fail

he. he go about a good deal, and be always eager for new thing

because they save he from boredom, the malign parent of much needless

activity. he have an expensive little cottage near Rye in Kent to which

he could motor without needless trouble whenever London bore he, and

directly his cottage bore he he would come back to London. and he

visit people’s house a lot because it be too troublesome to resist

invitation.



there be, it must be admit, limit to the wisdom of Paul Lambone.

it be often more difficult to see what be near we than what be far

away; many a stout fellow who look with a clear, discern eye upon

the universe see little of his toe, and ignore the intervene

difficulty; and something sub-conscious in Paul Lambone’s mind

obstinately refuse to recognize the defective nature of many of his

private act. he know he be indolent, but he would not allow himself

to admit that his indolence be fundamental and vicious. he think

there be a Paul Lambone in reserve of very great energy. he like to

think of himself as a man of swift and accurate decision who, once

arouse, be capable of demoniac energy. he have spend many an hour

in arm-chair, on garden seat, and on Downland turf, think out

his course of action on various possible occasion of war, business,

criminal attempt or domestic crisis. his favourite hero in real life

be Napoleon, Julius Cæsar, Lord Kitchener, Lord Northcliffe, Mr.

Ford, and suchlike heroic ant.



he like Christina Alberta because of her tremendous go. she be always

up to something; she prefer stand to sit, and she kick she

leg about while she talk to you. he idealize she go; he attribute

to her much more go than she really have. he be secretly persuade that

her blood must be like a bird’s, a degree or so above normal. he feel

that in imagination she have much in common with he. he call she the

last thing, the Van, the Ultimate Modern Girl, and the Life Force. he

openly profess pity for the unaided single-handed man who would in

accordance with our social law presently have to marry she and go she

pace and try to keep she in order.



she have be to tea with he once or twice. she perceive his

admiration and suspect a certain affection, and she bask in

admiration and affection. she like his book and think he be very

like what he think he be himself. she tell he all sort of thing

about herself just to lift his eyebrow.



and he be wise all over she and round and about she, tremendously wise.





§ 2



it be quite interesting to be ring up by Christina Alberta and ask

if she might come for tea and advice. “come along now,” he say. “I’m

probably all alone for tea.”



and as he replace the instrument he say: “now I wonder what the young

woman have be up to! and what she want I to do for she.”



he go back to his sitting-room and spread himself on his very nice

persian hearthrug, and regard the pretty silver kettle that swing

over his spirit lamp. “it willn’t be money,” he consider. “she ben’t

the sort that try to get money....



“she’s bark her shin on something....



“girl nowadays be a lot too plucky--they’re a lot too plucky

altogether.... I hope it’s nothing serious.... she’s just a kid.”



Christina Alberta appear in due course. erect as ever, but

nevertheless look a little dash and subdue.



“Uncle,” she say--for that be her theory of their relationship--“I’m

in trouble. you’ve get to give I all sort of advice.”



“take off that brigand’s cloak,” he say, “and sit down there and make

Christina Alberta pause with her cloak in her hand and stare at he.

“you know my daddy,” she say, arm akimbo.



“I never see a more dissimilar parent.”



“Well--” she consider how she should put it. “he’s behave queerly.

so that people may think--people who don’t know he--that he’s go

Mr. Lambone reflect. “be it ever such a very serious mind to go out

of?”

what I want just now.”



“but ben’t there a man,” say Lambone, “on whom you have a sort of

“you know who it be, I suppose?”



he be frank. “thing rather show.”



“if you know him--” she leave the sentence unfinishe.



“I know the young man only very incidentally,” he say.



“I go to Teddy,” say Christina Alberta without any further

“so it’s get to that,” Paul Lambone reflect with hidden dismay, and

then remark a little belatedly: “not every lover.”



“mine--anyhow.”



“and you come away?”



“_well!_ what do _you_ think?”



“H’m,” say Lambone. “you _have_ bark your shin, Christina Alberta!

More than I think.”



“oh, Hell take Teddy!” say Christina Alberta, put it on a bit and

help herself by be noisy. “what do that matter now? I’ve do

“well, first you’ve get to tell I all about it,” say Lambone.

“because at present, you know, I’ve hardly get the hang of the trouble.

he be. blue eye. and he be talk--what nonsense _be_ he talk?

about the Lost Atlantis. but it be quite nice nonsense.... no, don’t

interrupt. just let I recall my own impression of he until you’ve have

your tea.”





§ 3



when the tea be make and Christina Alberta have sip a cup and look

tell she she might begin.



“he’s get queer in his mind, but you know that he ben’t really

“that _be_ it,” say Christina Alberta. “you see--” she pause.



Lambone sit down in a second arm-chair and sip his tea in a

“he’s a person of peculiar imaginativeness. he always have be. always.

he’s always live half in a dream. we’ve be very much together ever

all such wonderful, impossible, far-off thing. the further off the

my play come very near to believe. I could princess it for a whole

“I do,” say Lambone. “for day together I would be a great indian

chief, sentence to death again and again--disguise as a small

do it more or less for a time.”



“but he’s go on do it all his life. and he’s do it now more

“they _be_ in a bad way,” say Lambone. “People don’t begin to know

“I’m afraid, yes.”



“soon?”



“that’s what worry I.



“you see,” she go on, “I’m afraid he’s go to strike most people

“he and his daughter get upset about daddy’s queerness. they frighten

“and now he’s in London?”



“he have a sort of idea he’s overlord of the king, and he want to go to

“rather like Muhammad’s letter to the potentate,” say Lambone.

“untidy in his dress?”



“neat as ever.”



“I remember when I see he how neat he be. be he--at all--incoherent?

“it’s just one simple delusion? he have no delusion about have great

“and he be now?”



“yes.”



“let’s hope that last. I don’t see that a man be insane because he

“yes, I know,” say Lambone. “Hate.”



“yes,” she say. “Hate.”



she jump to her foot and take possession of the hearthrug, look

“no,” say Lambone.



for some second--it seem quite a long time to Christina Alberta--he

“who willn’t let anything happen?”



“yes.”



“so far, good.”

“I ought to come and see he. now.”

a great tranquillity descend upon he.



she could not restrain a faint exclamation of impatience.

§ 4



the taxi drop they at the entrance of the Mews.

but when they reach the studio a little surprise await they. fay

her conviction be all too manifest that he would never come back.

§ 5



Lambone follow Christina Alberta into the studio and sit down at once

“I’m at your service.”

they find a taxi in the King’s Road. Christina Alberta do not

§ 6

_BOOK the second_



the WORLD reject SARGON, king of king









CHAPTER the FIRST



_Incognito_





§ 1



Mr. Preemby have disappear from Christina Alberta’s world. for a time

he must disappear almost as completely from this story. Mr. Preemby

fade out. take over his outward likeness we have now to tell of

another and great person, Sargon the First, the Magnificent One, king

of all king, the Inheritor of the Earth.



it be no doubt a very wonderful and glorious thing to discover that

instead of be the rather obscure widower of a laundry proprietor

with no particular purpose in the world one be Lord of the Whole

World, but it be also, to a conscientious man anxious to do right,

an extremely disturbing and oppressive discovery. and at first it be

natural that there should be something a little confusing to the mind

in this vast and glittering idea. it be an idea that carry with it

an effect of release and enlargement. for purpose as yet obscure, he

have be catch like a cage creature in that limited and uninteresting

Preemby life. his imagination have rebel against its finality; some

deep instinct have warn he that his life be an illusion; in moment

of reverie, and sometimes between sleep and wake, there have be

intimation of a light and purpose beyond the apparent reality. now

abruptly, as though a portal swung open, as though a curtain be draw,

that light pour upon he dazzlingly. his be no single life that

begin and end and be do with like an empty song. his existence

be like a thread that shine and vanish and return in the unending

fabric of be; that be weave into a purpose. in the past he have

be Porg in the city of Kleb, and he have be Sargon and Belshazzar.

many other have he be, but those memory still slumber under the

dark water of forgetfulness. but the memory of Sargon shine bright.

it be his Sargon self that have return and no other of his self.

for some reason that be still obscure the power that rule his life

need he to be Sargon once again, in this great distressful world of

to-day. Sargon have begin life humbly as an outcast babe and have rise

to restore and rule and extend a mighty empire than the old world

have ever see before. certain quality (h’rrmp) have be display by

Sargon, and because of these quality the Power have call upon he

again.



a whole series of memory be unfold in his mind. with an

extraordinary convincingness he recall his youth, memory of those

distant day in Sumeria where the Power have set he on high. they

be so bright and so pleasing that already they be thrust his

recollection of Sheringham and Woodford Wells into the background,

make a fading phantom of his Preemby existence. he have never

cherish these latter experience, never turn they over in his

mind with any pleasure. but his newly-restore memory be memory

to dwell upon. there be picture of his early life when he be a

foundling, a mysterious foundling at the court of his predecessor.

(that predecessor’s name still escape he.) Young Sargon be a fair

and blue-eyed youth, a thing rare in brown Sumeria, and he have be

find float down the great river in a little cradle boat of rush

and bituman, and he have be take and adopt by the ruler of the

land. and already as a lad he be point out as have an exceptional

wisdom, as be able to do what other man could not do, he have the

genius of the ruler. it be not that he have great cleverness nor great

skill nor strength. many of those about he excel he in these minor

thing--in cleverness and memory work in particular, Prewm, the son of

the Grand Vizier, excel he--but he have the true, the kingly wisdom

beyond they all.



“the true, the kingly wisdom,” say Mr. Preemby-Sargon aloud and

come into sharp collision with a tall, dark gentleman hurrying from

St. James’s Park towards St. James’s Palace. “sorry!” cry Mr.

Preemby-Sargon.



“my fault,” say the tall, dark gentleman. “late for an appointment.”

and sweep on.



Odd! where have Mr. Preemby--or Sargon--see that face before? could it

be--something connect it vaguely with Sheringham and sunlit sand. and

then Sumeria come uppermost and the tall, dark gentleman become the

chieftain of a desert tribe, a desert tribe amidst the sand.



the ruffled surface become calm again, and that boyhood in Sumeria

resume possession of the mirror. where be we? even in those early

day man have mark a gravity in the lad beyond his year. he have

avoid puerile game. in all his life he have avoid puerile game.

no cricketer even at Sheringham. modestly but firmly this youth have

raise his voice in the council chamber and his word be see to be

wisdom. the old man have sit around marvelling. “he sayeth Sooth,” they

say in their antique sumerian way. a soothsayer. he be also call

the Young Pathfinder.



before Sargon be fifteen the old childless ruler, beset by enemy,

threaten with plot, have mark he. “this boy might save the state.”

then, while yet only a few day over eighteen he be give charge of an

expedition to pacify the mountain folk of the north and persuade they

not to ally themselves with the Enemy of the North. he do more than

he be tell to do. he go through the mountain land into the plain

beyond, and give battle to the Enemy of the North and defeat he and

smote he severely. after that all man perceive that he must be the

new Lord and Master of Sumeria, the successor of his aged Patron. all

applaud with sincerity except Prewm the Clever (already adorn with

precocious sprout whisker) and he applaud with envy in his eye.

and then come the day of the succession, and the Inauguration of the

Harem, beautiful day. and after that the birth of the Princess Royal,

his only child, and that great expedition into the desert of the

south. and more expedition and great law-making, wise law and wiser,

and crowd of applauding, grateful people and happy village. life

become happy universally. Prewm plot and rebel and be deal with

after the simple custom of the time. that be a regrettable necessity,

a thing not to be dwell upon. the frontier spread and the great

peace spread; Russia, Turkey in Europe, Persia, India, Ancient Egypt,

Somaliland, and so on and so forth, be conquer and make happy.

America and Australia and the remain of Atlantis, still not completely

submerge, be discover. they be forget again afterwards, but

they be really discover then--and pay tribute. a League of Nations

be establish.



all the world tell of the goodness of Sargon and live golden day. for

Sargon rule by the light of justice in his heart. he mitigate the

sacrifice in the Temples and introduce a kind of Protestantism into

the creed and service. the people make song praise he. pass

man and woman would run to kiss his hand. nor do he deny himself to

his people. that confidence be his end. come alas! the assassin’s

knife, the black assassin, a madman, a stranger----!



it be wonderful. he could remember his people mourn after he be

dead.



the white glove of a policeman against Mr. Preemby-Sargon’s chest

just save he from step in front of a motor ’bus. he recoil

dexterously. he be in Trafalgar Square, a great meeting-place, a

confluence. here in the warm light of the October afternoon be a crowd

even great than the sumerian crowd. here he would observe they.

they be dark crowd, anxious-face crowd. his come back have to do

with they. there have be a great war, much devastation; the world be

wound and unable to recover. the poor ruler and politician of this

age have no wisdom, have no instinct for the fundamentally right thing.

once more a leader and a saviour be require, one who have the wisdom

that count.



it be Sargon that walk under the nose of Nelson’s lion and make

his way past the statue of George the Fourth to the balustrading that

command the square. he take up his position there for a long survey.

he look down whitehall to the great tower of the Houses of Parliament

and Whitehall be full of a golden haze set with a glitter of traffic.

the stream of omnibus, car, and motor van pour up to mingle with

the stream that come out of Northumberland Avenue and the Strand, and

the joint flow sunder again to the left of he and to the right away

to Pall Mall, across the square there. no street lamp be light as yet,

but in the rounded bluff of build to the left a few window be

warm with light. below, across the square, thin stream of pedestrian

flow like ant from one point to another and the squat little

station of the Tube Railway perpetually swallow up dot and clump

of individual. some sort of meeting be go on at the foot of the

Nelson column, a mere knot of people without evident enthusiasm. man

§ 2

“and now I can tell you,” say Bobby, when at last all three of they

be sit at table.



“it’s a little dear of a lunatic,” he say. “if it’s a lunatic at all.”



“I hope it ben’t,” say Tessy.



“he’s quite tidy in his person and he hang together--mentally he hang

together--and his eye ben’t in the least wild. a leetle too bright

and open perhaps. but he _do_ think the world belong to he.”



“well, Susan do that,” say Billy.



“and so do Billy,” say Tessy.



“but not quite with the same air of magnificent responsibility. you see

of he because we have a little dust-up with the Turks round and about

his stamp ground--he think he’s this Sargon come again and come

somehow--that bit’s a little difficult--into the empire of the whole

earth. it be Sargon, you know, who start all these british Lions and

Imperial Eagles in the world. and so he propose to take possession of

the planet, which be in such a frightful mess----”



“hear, hear!” say Billy.



“and put it in order.”



“what more simple?” cry Tessy.



“exactly. why haven’t we _all_ think of that?” say Billy.



“but where do he come from?” ask Tessy.



“not a sign. he might be a suburban nurseryman or a small draper or

something of that sort. I cann’t place he. one or two phrase he

use suggest a house-agent. but he may have pick they up from

advertisement. and his first proceeding--perfectly rational--be to

buy a map of the world. naturally if you be go to rule the world,

you ought to have a map of it handy.”



“have he any money?”



“enough to pay his way. he’s get a small note-case. that seem to be

all right. after we’d get a bag for he--he haven’t any luggage, and

I think he’d well buy a bag to satisfy Mrs. Richman--we sit down

upstairs and have a simple straightforward talk about the world and what

have to be do with it. most instructive.”



Bobby help himself to some more fried potato.



“and what _be_ go to be do with it?”



“let I see,” say Bobby; “it’s quite an attractive little programme.

rather I think on the line of the Labour programme. only simple and

more thorough. the Distinction of Rich and Poor be to be abolish

altogether. woman be to be free from all Disadvantages. there be to

be no More War. he get at the root of thing every time.”



“if those be the root,” say Billy.



“but ben’t the real question how to do it?” ask Tessy, “ben’t we all

agree about those thing--in theory?”



“in theory, yes,” say Bobby. “but not in reality. if every one really

want to abolish the difference of rich and poor it would be as easy

as pie to find a way. there’s always a way to everything if you want

to do it enough. but nobody really want to do these thing. not as we

want meal. all sort of other thing people want, but want to have

no rich and poor any more ben’t real wanting; it be just a matter of

pious sentiment. and so it be about war. we don’t want to be poor and

we don’t want to be hurt or worry by war, but that’s not want to

end those thing. _he_ want to end they.”



“but how’s he go to set about it?” say Billy.



“that be still a little vague. I think there be to be a sort of

Proclamation. he’s think that out upstairs now. he seem to

think that he have to call up some sort of disciple. then I think

he will go down to Westminster and take the Speaker’s place on the

Woolsack or something of that sort. it _be_ the Speaker sit on the

Woolsack, ben’t it? or be it the Lord Chancellor? anyhow, there be

to be demonstration--on a large and dignified scale. the people have

forget the ancient simple law, he say. he himself have forget

they. but he have remember they now; they have come back to he, and

presently every one will remember they, all the great old thing,

justice, faith, obedience, mutual service. as it be in Ancient

Sumeria. Dreamland Ancient Sumeria, you know. the dear old Golden

Age. he have come to remind people of the true thing in life--which

every one have forget. and when he have remind they every one will

remember. and be good. and there you be, Tessy! I hope it will affect

Susan--but I don’t feel at all sure. I have a sort of feeling that

Susan could stash up any golden age they start in about five minute,

but then perhaps I’m a bit prejudiced about she.”



“it’s rather wonderful all the same,” say Billy.



“he _be_ rather wonderful. he sit up there and look at you with

his little round innocent face and tell you all these thing. and,

assume he’s Lord of the World, they’re perfectly right and proper.

he sit with the map of the world unroll on the table. I venture

to suggest that he would require a lot of subordinate ruler and

director. ‘they will come,’ he say. ‘no office, no duty shall be do

henceforth except by the Proper People. that have be neglect too

long. let every man do that he be well fit to do. then everything

will be well.’”



“and when’s all this to begin?” ask Billy.



“any time. I don’t think--” Bobby’s expression become profoundly

out to he that he ought to consider his demonstration very carefully

before he make they, and he seem inclined to agree. some mistake,

he say, have occur already--I couldn’t find out what. apparently

to-morrow he be just go to look at London, quietly but firmly,

to observe the manner of his subject in the street and railway

have fall from his eye, he explain, and now that he know that he

be Lord of the World he realize how distressed and unsatisfactory

everybody’s life be--even when they don’t know it’s unsatisfactory. his

own life be terribly unsatisfactory he say, unreal and frustrated,

until he awake and realize the greatness of his destiny.”



“and what be that life of his?” ask Tessy.



“I try to get that. and he catch the eager note in my voice I

suppose, and shut up like an oyster. the draper’s shop perhaps. or a

milk-round. I wonder.”



“but, Bobby,” cry Billy in the expostulatory tone of one who

remonstrate with wild unreason; “he must come from _somewhere_!”



“exactly,” say Bobby. “and somewhere must be look for he. but

you cann’t always hurry these thing. and meanwhile I take it it’s

our bounden duty to keep an eye on he and see that he don’t bump

himself too hard or fall into the wrong hand.... until some one come

along.... don’t you get up, Tessy, you’re tired.”



and Bobby jump to his foot and proceed to change the plate and

dish while Billy fall into a profound meditation upon the problem of

the new neighbour upstairs.



presently he smile and shake his head in kindly disapproval.



“this will suit Bobby all right,” he say to Tessy. “whenever he ought

to be upstairs in his own room get on with that novel of his he

will be up on the second floor talk to--what be it?--Sargon.”



“accumulate material,” correct Bobby from the Welsh dresser where

he be turn a tin of peach out into a glass dish. “accumulate

evidence.... not even a spider could spin a yarn out of an empty

stomach. Tessy, have you just simply _lose_ the cream or have you put

it away in a new place very carefully for I to guess? oh, right-o!

I’ve get it.”

let we be frank about it; Sargon have his doubt.



not always. there be time when his fantasy be bravely alive and

yet even when Sargon doubt he believe. it be a very comprehensible

so he walk up and down his little upper room in Midgard Street and

the word “crisp” have settle it.



“I will avail myself,” say Sargon.



“and it’s equally easy to find your way back,” say Bobby with a faint

“have no fear, young man,” say Sargon in a tone of jolly reassurance.

“of course,” say Bobby. “I forget that.

“how fair it might be. how great it might be!

he be no long trouble by his oblivion of the sumerian tongue.

“H’rrmp,” he say, a word common to many tongue, clear the way

CHAPTER the second



_the calling of the Disciples_





§ 1



there be already wide difference of statement and opinion about

the order and the detail of the calling of the follower of Sargon.

happily we be in a position to give the circumstance with all the

exactitude that may be necessary, and with an authority that will

anticipate vexatious criticism. it be about half-past six that Sargon

appear in Cheapside, and the day’s traffic be already ebb in that

fresh-complexion, sincere and innocent countenance speak clearly

it go along Cheapside scrutinize the face of the foot passenger

on a momentous and mystical search. the blue eye beneath the brim of

the distinguished-look hat be alight. be it to be this man? be

it to be this?

the moral of a proverb depend entirely upon the image choose, and

§ 3



but that be exactly what our dear Sargon do not know. by this time he

up to the beginning of the calling of the disciple Sargon have rule

since first mankind in its slow ascent from mere animalism become

CHAPTER the THIRD



_the Journey of Sargon underneath the World_





§ 1



it have be say in an early chapter that Mr. Preemby, after his

and unexpected thing. a new phase in that belate germination begin as

§ 5



Sargon sit up in bed, motionless now except that his head turn slowly

he do not look beyond the come night. that alone he foresee as an

he know that this harsh, naked electric light which prize up his

the long night must end at last.



then suddenly Sargon, between sleep and waking, see something

what be the mad and what do madness mean?



why have he be lift up by the Supreme Power, out of the common act

and imagination of his daily life, to a knowledge of his immortal

whole world as his sphere, merely to be cast out of life and light and

and then the wind of a second question blow across his mind. the

the whole scheme of thing in Sargon’s mind begin to shudder and

blankly at the last black possibility.



be the whole of this call and this mission of his a deception and a

delusion? have he be cheat at Tunbridge Wells when the call first

but could he go back?



yes. that could be do. so easily! he could say plainly that now

he remember his proper name. he could ask to see the doctor or the

director or whoever it be preside over Jordan and Higgs and their

he think of that alert, kind, slightly antagonistic figure. if only

release....



then he would be just Preemby again for the rest of his day,

comfortably Preemby, Preemby the bystander, Preemby the onlooker, the

ineffective, speechless man in the background of the noisy studio. but

certain thing would be at an end. he would never go to any museum

tale and fancy of thing that would never arrive. past and future

never be before. the bladder of his life would be prick....



and the other way lie pain, indignity, rough treatment, vile food,

filthy circumstance, trial that might break he--but with the power

he think of the thing that belong to Sargon; of the power, of

city that be like great single person, of the Whole World, of the

mystical promise of the star, of all these thing he must renounce

definite in his mind. and at last he speak. “no,” he say in a hoarse

voice that be almost a shout. “I be Sargon, Sargon the servant of

God--and the Whole World be mine!”
