long after midnight Sargon be still sit up in that bleak greasy

glare amidst the noise and disorder about he. he have lose all count

of time; his watch have be take from he. somewhen in the small hour

he be pray. and at time he weep a little.



he pray. sometimes he make sentence and whisper they to himself

and sometimes the sentence never get to word but pass through his

mind like serpent that be see through deep, dark water. “Great be

the task thou hast put upon I. I see now I be not worthy, o Master,

to do the least thing that be require of I. I be not worthy. I be a

petty man and a foolish man and all I have do so far be folly. but

thou hast call I, know my folly. forgive thou my folly and help

thou my faith.” silent and still he sit with tear upon his cheek.

“any punishment and any trial,” he whisper at last, “only that thou

shouldst not desert I and vanish out of my world.”



he pray that the power would yet make he the servant of the world

and add falteringly and feebly, “even as it be in the ancient day.”



for have there even be those ancient day? Sumeria be now very far

away from he, and the white town and the blue river and the river

galley have fade and the worship crowd be just a faint smear

upon his mind no strange than the fade memory of a dream. he say

nothing for a time, and then in a very loud whisper he say: “help thou

my unbelief.”



sometimes he pray in a whisper and sometimes he pray silently

and sometimes he sit quite still. Higgs come and look at he once

or twice but do not interfere with he. the poem in the adjacent

bed still go on, but it have now become a mere rhyme stream of

blasphemous obscenity.



for a time after he have make an end to his praying, Sargon must have

sleep; he must have sleep because he be awaken by the dawn. it do

not come gradually; he awake to it.



a cold, shadowless light fill the ward and the electric lamp that

have seem so bright be just luminous orange-yellow thread. and

Higgs be stand in the doorway peer intently at the red-haired

man, who be lie with his head on the table as though he be

asleep--but perhaps only pretend to be asleep.





§ 7



it be on the afternoon of the following day that two stranger come to

see Sargon. he be take to they and they talk a little with he, and

then chiefly with each other. Higgs be off duty but Jordan hover in

the background.



neither of these gentleman explain their business with Sargon to he.

one of they be a short man in a black coat; he wear a gold watch-chain

and a rich-look tie with a jewel pin; he have a gold pince-nez, a

little pointed nose, a fat, clean-shaven white face, and a mouth like

an oblique spade-thrust in a lump of dough. he speak with something

between a sniff and a lisp, and he be evidently rather in a hurry and

annoy at having be call in to see Sargon. the other be large and

grey and wear-look; he impress Sargon in some indefinable way as

be a medical man who have private trouble. he seem to consider

himself in charge of the conversation, and would occasionally refer

upon some point of fact to the hovering Jordan.



“understand,” say the pasty-face man, “you want to give some sort

of dinner party to all sort of people. Eh?--at the Rubicon. I suppose

it come on you sort of sudden like. eh?”



“I wish to confer with certain people,” say Sargon. “it may have

be a mistake on my part.”



“no doubt it be a mistake, Mr.--Mr.----”



“willn’t ’ave a Mister to it,” say Jordan from the background. “call

’isself Sargon.”



the doctor become very acute in his manner. “now ben’t that some sort

of historical name?” he ask with a sideways searching look.



“it be,” say Sargon.



“but it ben’t _your_ name, you know.”



“possibly not. I mean--it be my only name.”



“that’s a bit of an answer, that be,” say the pasty-face man. “my

_word_!”



“what be your _real_ name?” ask the doctor persuasively.



“Sargon.”



“not Mr. A. E. Preemby?”



Sargon start and stare, possibly with a certain wildness in his

eye. “with God’s help, _no_!” he say.



“be it ever Mr. Preemby?” ask the doctor.



“that do not matter now. that be of no importance now.”



“it may be of _some_ importance,” say the pasty-face man.



“and now you’re a King or a Lord or something and you own the world?”

say the doctor.



Sargon make no answer. he feel he be in a net.



the doctor turn to Jordan and beckon he in a whisper. only one

sentence come to Sargon’s ear. “’iggs ’eard it,” say Mr. Jordan.



“ben’t you call Sargon the Magnificent?” ask the doctor.



Sargon bend his head in sorrow. “well be it to call I Sargon the

Unworthy. for in many thing I have fail.”



the pasty-faced man look at the doctor. “haven’t we have about enough

of this?”



“my conclusion be clear,” say the doctor. “in fact, I have the

certificate ready.”



“if you’re satisfied, Dr. Manningtree, I be. if I’m to see those other

feller.”



“I’ve get all the paper in my room,” say the doctor.



“right-o,” say the pasty-face man.



“it’s very good of you to come to-day. I wouldn’t have bother you

until to-morrow but we be really get overcrowded here. one chap’s

decidedly dangerous. the attendant here don’t like the look of he.

you need only just see he for a moment. or any of they. all clear

case for summary reception order.”



they speak now as if Sargon be not present or as if he be an

inanimate object. and indeed for they he have become so; he have pass

for they already out of the comity of mankind.



“why have you be talk to I?” ask Sargon suddenly with a vague

fear of what have be say and do develop in his mind.



the doctor’s manner alter. he speak to Sargon as one might speak to a

small child. “you’ll be go back to bed now,” he say. “Jordan!”



“but I want to know.”



“go with Mr. Jordan.”



“what be these paper you speak about?”



the doctor turn his back on Sargon without an answer and the man with

the pasty face open the door to depart. Sargon make a step towards

they, but Jordan lay hold of his arm.



and while Sargon be be steer back to bed, firmly rather than

gently, in the grip of Jordan, the justice and the doctor fill in and

sign the form that be necessary to deprive he of nearly every

right he possess as a human being. for there be no trial by jury

and no writ of habeas-corpus in Britain for the unfortunate charge

with insanity. he may not plead in public and there be no one to whom

he may appeal. he may write complaint but they will be neglect;

his most urgent expostulation will be disregard in favour of any

dull attendant’s asseveration. he be hand over to the nearly

autocratic control of under-educate, ill-pay, ill-fed, and overwork

attendant. every night and every day seem endless to he at first,

and then the night and day fall into a sort of routine and become

unimportant and pass away more and more rapidly. he be almost always

keep in a state of bodily discomfort, always rather ill from the

ill-prepared and sometimes taint food, and much incommode by clumsy

drugging and particularly by the administration of violent purgative.

in croton oil alone be our asylum truly generous. he have excellent

reason for fear many of his fellow inmate and for a servile

obedience to the attendant in charge of he. a medical superintendent

hover in the background; a medical staff with no special training in

mental science. they pass through the ward at the appoint time,

avoid trouble, see as little as possible.



and, after all, what can they do? they cannot raise the expenditure

upon food or increase the number or salary of the attendant.

they be appoint to save and not to spend the ratepayer’ money.

the attendant work together and protect each other; they must hang

together; many of they go in fear of the violent case. occasionally,

after due notice, a visit magistrate will pay a formal visit to the

asylum. everything be put in order for the occasion. the inmate with a

grievance dares not accost he or do not know how to accost he nor

how to frame his complaint. the attendant be at hand to interrupt,

embarrass, and explain. so, with no possibility of redress, the poor

half-lunatic will be roughly handle, badly feed, and coarsely clothe,

and night and day he will have no other familiar company than the

insane. it be bad enough for the sane to be afflict by the vagary,

the violence, the exasperate mechanism, the incoherence of the

truly demented, but what must it be for those upon whom the penumbra

of that same shadow have fall? they have no privacy; no escape from

those other; no peace. our world herd these discard together out of

sight, wall they up, spend so little upon they that they be neither

properly feed nor properly look after, and do its brave hopeful good

to forget all about they.



and our Sargon, who even in the outer world of usage and freedom be

sometimes a little at a loss, must now go on into this dark underworld.

for two day more he will be keep in the Gifford Street Observation

Ward await the convenience of the authority; then in the company

of four other prisoner he will be send to a still bleaker and more

desolate and hopeless confinement within the cluster building and

wall and railing of Cummerdown Hill.



so he pass now for a time out of sight of everyday mankind, and

so also for a time he shall pass out of this story. it would be

insufferable to tell with any fulness his daily tale of discomfort and

indignity.









_BOOK the THIRD_



the resurrection of SARGON, king of king









CHAPTER the first



_Christina Alberta in Search of a Father_





§ 1



Hitherto Christina Alberta have face life with a bold, disdainful, and

successful gesture. the discretion and scruple of other be not

for she. she have see no reason for their prudent hesitation, their

convention and restraint. now for the first time she know dismay. her

daddy have vanish into a world that she suddenly realize could be

immensely cruel. Teddy be rotten, so plainly rotten that only a fool

wrap up in her own sensation would have touch he. she lie awake

for most of the night after her daddy have vanish, bite her hand

and damn Teddy. lambone the great friend be lazy, incompetent, and

futile. Harold and Fay seem already a little tired of her misfortune

and vaguely disposed to blame she for bring her daddy to London. she

have no one else to whom she could turn. no one remain to she--except

Christina Alberta herself, feel a little soil now and more than a

little afraid. “but what be I to do?” she ask the night, again and

again, in her stuffy but artistic little bedroom.



among the other disadvantage of her position she have less than a pound

of ready money.



it have to be record that for two whole day Christina Alberta do

not even take the obvious step of go to the police. she have a queer

instinctive knowledge of the danger of bring the police and the

social system generally to bear upon her odd little daddy. she have

an innate distrust of official human being. it be Paul Lambone who

induce she to go to the police. he have the grace to be ashamed of his

unhelpfulness, and after an interval of two clear day he come round to

the Lonsdale Mews to offer his generous but sluggish help once more. he

catch she have tea with Fay.



“Christina Alberta,” he say, look a very large comfortable figure

of sorrow, “I’ve be worry and worry about you all the time. I

don’t help enough. I think he’d come back of his own accord and that

all the fuss be a little premature. have you hear anything?”



Christina Alberta be tear between the desire to snap his head off

and the realization that he be, after his fashion, quite sincerely

friendly to she and could be very useful.



“_say_ it!” say Lambone. “you’ll be well, my dear, after you’ve say

it and then we can talk matter over.”



he get the reassurance of a smile. he brighten visibly. he be the

sort of man who would hate to feel hate even by a cockroach. “I willn’t

sit in that chair, thank,” he say to Fay. “it’s too comfortable. and

at any moment we may think of something, and I may have to leap up and

act.



“Spartan,” he say, sit down.



“Eh?” say Fay.



“Spartan. my doctor tell I to say it before every meal and especially

before tea. I don’t know why. Magic or Coué or something. be these

thing cocoanut cake? I think so.... good they be. and what be we

go to do about it, Christina Alberta?”



he become sane and helpful and more and more like the man who write

_what to do on a hundred and one Occasions_. he make Christina Alberta

admit to bankruptcy, and make it clear to she that it be her duty to

accept a loan of twenty-five pound from he. he then deal with the

point about report the disappearance to the police, and convince

Christina Alberta upon that. if Mr. Preemby have fall into bad hand

the soon the police look for he the well. but he do not think

that likely; he be much more inclined to the idea that Preemby have

make a disturbance and be take up. he guess he would be deal with

as a mental case. he have consult those useful book _the Justice

of the Peace_ and the _Encyclopædia Britannica_, and display the

excellence of his mental digestion. Christina Alberta perceive that he

have in he the making of a competent barrister.



he carry off Christina Alberta in a taxi to Scotland Yard. “either

they’ll tell we there or tell we where we shall be tell,” he say.



Fay be impress by the originality of that. “now if it be a lost

umbrella,” she say, “I could understand it. but I’d never have think

of go to Scotland Yard for a lost father.”



by six Mr. Preemby have be trace to Gifford Street. but there be no

see he at Gifford Street. he have be certify as a lunatic and he

be bind, the attendant thought but ben’t quite sure, for Cummerdown

Hill. Paul Lambone try to be dignified and important and to prevail

over the attendant and extract further information, but not very

successfully. in the end he and Christina Alberta depart with little

more than one immense discouraging fact. they would not be able to see

Mr. Preemby nor to learn anything very material about his condition

until the next visit day, whenever that might be, at Cummerdown

Hill. then if he be “fit to be visit,” they might go and see he.

the attendant be rigid in his statement and have the air of dislike

both Lambone and Christina Alberta extremely.



as they come away from Gifford Street Christina Alberta observe that

Lambone be angry. she have never see he angry before. it be a very

transitory phase. there be an unusual depth of pink in his cheek.



“Dog in office,” he say. “just there to annoy people--anxious people.

one would think ... man in my position ... certain standing.... some

attention.... any other country but this, Man of letter have respect.”



Christina Alberta agree mutely.



“manner in a public official--primary.”



“he be detestable,” say Christina Alberta.



“not at the end of my resource,” say Lambone.



Christina Alberta wait.



“ought to have go to Devizes in the first place. know more about

mental case and lunacy law than any other man in London. wonderful

fellow. I’ll go back to my flat and I’ll ring he up and make an

appointment. then he’ll put we wise about the whole business. and I

want you to meet he anyhow. you’ll appreciate devize. come to think

of it, you’re remarkably like he.”



“how?”



“got the Life Force and all that. and physically like he. very. the

same nose--much the same profile.”



“it’s a nose more suitable for a man,” say Christina Alberta. “I

suppose _he_ carry it off all right!”



“it’s a damned good nose, Christina Alberta,” say Lambone. “it’s a

valiant nose. don’t you break down to any modesty about it. it be

your nose attract I first to you. you’ll spike a husband with it

yet, and he’ll adore it and follow it even as you do. nowadays woman

have to be free and individualized; they have get to have feature

and distinction. the day of the wanton curl and the swan neck and

complexion have go. not that you haven’t the clear skin I’ve ever

set eye upon, Christina Alberta.”



“tell I some more about Dr. Devizes,” say Christina Alberta.





§ 2



but it ben’t the next day that Christina Alberta meet Devizes. she put

that off for a day and flee down to Woodford Wells in response to a

remarkable communication from Mr. Sam Widgery.



the Widgerys have never be correspondent of the Preembys except in so

far as the payment of the dividend upon Mr. Preemby’s interest in the

Limpid Stream Laundry necessitate letter. there have be a certain

amount of friction in the flotation of the company, and Mr. Widgery have

remain resentful and seek to show as much by a study curtness

in his communication. but now come this letter address to “Miss

Chrissie Preemby.” “_my dear Chrissie_,” the letter begin.



“_this be a shocking business about your poor father I cann’t tell you

how shocked I be I go up post haste to the workhouse where you have

put he as soon as they write to I and get his watch and cheque-book.

it be fortunate they find my address in his pocket or I suppose I

should have be keep in the dark as per usual about all this he do

not know I and deny his own name but afterwards he say he know

I for a filching commercial wrogue and would have my ear cut off

and threaten I. I be to be unpailed whatever that may be. I have

be think over all this business and since you be not yet of age

I suppose I be a sort of guardian to you and have to look into your

interest at the laundry which be not pay nearly so well as your

father lead I to believe. I think he be already queer at the time and

don’t fully understand what he be do and I doubt if all that of

his about preference share which I never hold with really but do to

humour he ought to stand. luckily there be no hurry about this have you

willn’t have to pay anything for he where he be Mr. Punter say so long

as you leave well alone and we can see about these other thing when

you have get over the first shock of your father’s breakdown. my wife

send her love and kind sympathy. you must keep calm and not let these

thing disturb you too much because very often it be hereriterary and

one cannot be too careful so leave everything to I and believe I to

remain_



                                  “_your affectionate cousin_



                                                        “_Sam Widgery_.”



“rather!” say Christina Alberta and telephone forthwith to put off

Paul Lambone and Wilfred Devizes and hurry off with a combatant light

in her eye to the Underground and Liverpool Street Station.





§ 3



when Christina Alberta get down to Woodford Wells it seem to she

that the laundry be just a little small than it use to be and

that a slight tarnish have fall upon the bright blue delivery van.

the swastika upon they have be cover over by paper bill in

red lettering say: “under Entirely New Management. address all

communication to the Managing Director Samuel Widgery Esq. by Order.”



she go up the garden path to the door that have be home to she for

nearly all her life, and it be open to she by Sam Widgery himself,

who have see she come. “so you come down,” he say, and seem to

hesitate about admit she. he be a tallish, stoop man with

a large bare pock-mark face, a drop nether lip, a large nose

that snore occasionally when he breathe, and furtive very small

brown eye. he be clothe in dark grey ill-fitting garment, with a

fray collar and a very worn make-up black satin tie. his waistcoat

be mainly unbuttoned and he fidget with his hand. he look at

Christina Alberta as though he find she more formidable than he have

think she.



“do you see daddy?” say Christina Alberta, come straight to the

point.



he compress his mouth and shake his head from side to side as though

he recall painful thing.



“be he bad? be he queer or--_dreadful_?”



“not too loud, my dear,” he say in his husky whispering voice. “we

don’t want _every one_ to hear about your trouble. come in here where

we can talk properly.”



he lead the way into the little living-room in which her father have so

recently plan the condition for the conversion of the laundry into

a limited company. the familiar furniture have be rearrange rather

amazingly, and a large, dark bureau have be place under the window.

with discreet gesture, Sam Widgery close the door and come towards

she. “sit down, Chrissie,” he say, “and don’t get excited. I be

afraid you might come rush down here. but of course I be bind to

write to you.”



“do you see he?” she repeat.



“mad as a hatter,” he say. “they say he make a riot at the Rubicon

Restaurant. want to give a great banquet there to all the beggar in

London.”



“do you see he? be he well? be he unhappy? what have they do with

he?”



“you mustn’t _pelt_ I with question, Chrissie. you mustn’t let your

mind go on at such a pace. I tell you in my letter I go up and see

he. they call he out and he come to I in a little room.”



“where be it? where be this workhouse?”



“there you go again. just sit down and take thing quietly, my girl. I

cann’t answer all these question at once.”



“where be it you see he? be it at Gifford Street?”



“yes. where else could it be? they be wait to remove he.”



“where?”



“some asylum I suppose?”



“Cummerdown Hill?”



“come to think of it, it _be_ Cummerdown Hill. yes--they say

Cummerdown Hill. he come out. he look much as usual, but more vacant

perhaps, until he set eye on I and then he give a sort of start and

say, ‘don’t know you,’ he say. like that.”



“well, that ben’t mad. do he _look_ mad? I suppose he don’t want to

talk to you--after all the disagreeable thing that have be say.”



“very likely. Any’ow I say to he, ‘what! don’t know _I_?’ I say;

‘not know old Sam Widgery what you plant your laundry on?’ just like

that--joke at he like. quite kindly but--humorous. ‘I don’t know

you,’ he say and try to go. ‘hold hard!’ I say and take he by the

arm. ‘you’re a base, complain scoundrel,’ he say to I and sort of

try to push I away. ‘you’d rewin any laundry!’ he say--he to I,

what be in the laundry business a dozen year before he marry your

poor mother. ‘any ’ow I haven’t your complaint,’ I say, ‘Mr. Albert

Edward Preemby.’ he sort of stiffen. ‘sargum,’ he say, ‘if you

please....’”



“Sargon,” Christina Alberta correct.



“maybe. it sound like ‘Sargum.’ and ‘sargum’ he would have it. mad

as a hatter on that. I try to talk but what be the good of talk?

I couldn’t get anything plain or straight out of he at all. start

threaten I with the bastinado--whatever that may be. I ask he to

be decent with his language. ‘I’ve ’ad enough’ I say to the attendant

at last and the attendant take he away. and so we’re quit of he,

Chrissie.”



“quit of he!”



“quit of he. what can anyone do?”



“everything. do he look very unhappy? do he look frightened or

ill-treat?”



“why should he? they’re take proper care of he and he’s out of

harm’s way.”



“you’re sure he look--serene?”



“bit jade perhaps. that’s his own internal working I expect. but he’s

where he ought to be, Chrissie. I feel that. what we’ve get to do, from

what Mr. Punter say, be to let well alone. there he be with everything

he want, live on the ratepayer’ money. we’ve get ourselves to

consider. we’ve get to think of that crazy preference-share idea he’s

saddle the laundry with. _that’s_ urgent. it’s a charge of nearly five

hundred a year as thing be, nearly ten pound a week. there ben’t a

laundry in London could stand it.”



“I shall have to see my daddy,” say Christina Alberta. “I don’t

believe he _be_ so comfortable. I’ve hear horrid story of asylum.

anyhow, I ought to go right away and see he.”



“cann’t do that, Chrissie,” say Mr. Widgery, shake his large grey

face slowly from side to side and watch she as he speak. “they don’t

have visitor run in and out of these mad-house just whenever they

want to. wouldn’t _do_, you know. the poor creature have to be keep

quiet and not excited. I daresay I could give you a letter for next

visit day----”



“_you!_ give _I_ a letter!”



Mr. Widgery shrug his shoulder. “it would help you to get in. but

you willn’t make anything of he, Chrissie, even if you see he. and

you’ll have to wait for a visit day. you must do that.”



“I want to see he.”



“very likely. but regulation be regulation. meanwhile there’s all

this business muddle we get to put straight. while he’s in that asylum

I think what I ought to do be to give you ’n allowance, five pound

a week say, and keep back the rest until we’re able to get something

settle up. or four. or perhaps as you want it--not a definite sum. I

don’t know. I haven’t think it out yet. you cann’t possibly want to do

with all that ten pound a week with he off your hand. then we’ll be

able to see where we stand and everything will be all right again.”



he pause and scratch his cheek and watch she with his little

sidelong eye. “see?” he say as if to stimulate she to speech.



Christina Alberta look at he in a silence that become painful. then

she stand up and regard he--her arm akimbo and her face alight.



“I see now,” she say. “you damn old Rascal!”



Mr. Widgery have nice old-fashioned idea about young lady and

ladylike language. he be take aback. “now!” he say. “_now!_”



“always I should think,” say Christina Alberta.



“you mustn’t say thing like that, Chrissie. you mustn’t use word like

that. you mustn’t get wrong idea about all this. what d’you mean? old

rascal! something old rascal! why? I’m only do what I get to do. you

ben’t really of age yet, you’re just a mere child legally speak,

and it fall to _I_ naturally, _I_, his near relative so to speak,

to make such arrangement as have to be make about his affair. that’s

all. you mustn’t get wrong idea about it all and you mustn’t get

excited. see?”



“I call you,” say Christina Alberta, “a damned old Rascal.”



he do not meet her eye. he speak as if he appeal to the bureau.



“and do it do you any good to use such horrible expression? and do

it do I any harm? and do it alter the fact that whether I like it or

not, I’ve get to look after his property now and see about it and see

you don’t get into any trouble or mischief? all I and your aunt have

be think about be just what’s right and proper to be do for you.

and then you turn on I like a serpent and use language----!”



word fail Mr. Widgery, and still seek sympathy from the bureau,

he shrug his shoulder and throw out his hand.



“you’re no relation of his,” say Christina Alberta. “I guess when I

read your letter that this be the sort of thing you’d be up to. you’re

glad to have he out of the way because he be such a one for punctual

payment. you think I’m all alone; you think I’m just a girl and that

you can do what you like now with I. you’ve make a mistake. I’ll make

you pay up every penny that’s due on the laundry and I’ll see you do it

more punctually than ever. and why don’t you do something to get he

out as soon as you hear they’d take he?”



“now don’t you go get excite, Chrissie,” say Mr. Widgery. “even

if I ain’t a blood relation of his, I be of your. I’m your next-of-kin

and your good friend and I get to think what’s get to be do about

_you_. I get to act for _you_. in spite of bad language and everything.

I tell you he’s right and safe where he be and I’m not go to have

anything do to disturb he. nothing at all. there he be and there

he’s go to stay, and I’m go to act as Mr. Punter advise I to

act. I’m go to keep back his dividend as they fall due, and pay you

what I think proper to pay you for your keep and so on and charge it to

he, and I’m go to see you live an orderly sort of life in future

now too, same as my poor cousin Christina would have want you to do.

you’ve be knock about the world in a perfectly scandalous way and

learn to curse and swear. it cann’t go on. that’s how thing be,

Chrissie, and the soon you see they the right way round the well.”



the young woman stand speechless while Mr. Widgery unfold his

intention.



“where’s Mrs. Widgery?” she say at last with an effect of stupendous

self-control.



“up in the laundry seeing to thing. no need to bother she. we been

talk about all this, over and over, and we’re quite of one mind

in the matter. it’s _our_ right to look after you and it’s _our_

responsibility to look after you, and we mean to do our duty by you,

Chrissie, whether you like it or not.”



unpleasant doubt assail Christina Alberta. she be still two month

short of one and twenty, and it be quite conceivable that the law

give this ugly, oily creature all sort of preposterous power of

interference with she. still it be her style to carry off thing with

a brave face.



“all this be just nonsense,” she say. “I’m not go to have my daddy

put away like this without a word from I, and I’m not go to let you

muddle about with his property. everybody in the family know you’re

a crooked muddler. Mother use to say so. I’m go to see about he

_himself?_ not _herself_. in fact, a lover.

§ 5

phase, or rather two level of existence. roughly speak, two.

they have link and intermediate stage of course, but they could be

ignore when one just want to express the idea. first of all, he be

the old instinctive individual, fearful, greedy, lustful, jealous,

self-assertive. that be the primary self. he have to attend to that

primary self because it carry all the rest of he, as a rider

must see that his horse get oat. Deeper lie social instinct and

disposition arise out of family life. that be the second self, the

social self. man, he throw out, be a creature that have become more

and more consciously social in the last two or three hundred thousand

year. he have be lengthen his life, keep his child with he

long and long, enlarge his community from family herd into clan

and tribe and nation. the deep-lie continuity of life be become

more apparent and find more and more definite expression with this

socialization of man. to educate anyone in the proper sense of the word

be to make he more and more aware of this continuity. the importance

of the passionate feverish self be then reduce. true education be

self-subordination to a great life, to the social self. the natural

instinct and limitation of the primary self be in conflict with

this wide underflow; education, _good_ education, tend to correct

they.



“here be I,” say Devizes, “as we all be, a creature in a state of

internal conflict, quick, fiercer mortal instinct at issue with a

deep, calm, less brightly light, but finally strong drive towards

immortal purpose. and I be--how shall I put it?--I personally be, to

the good of my ability, on the side of the deep thing. my aptitude

and temperament and opportunity have bring I to psychology--as a

profession. I work to add to the accumulation of human knowledge and

understanding about the mind. I work for illumination. my particular

work be to study and cure troubled and tangle mind. I try to

straighten they out and simplify they and illuminate they. and above

all I try to learn from they. I seek the mental or physical cause

of their distress. I try to set down as clearly and accessibly as

possible, all I observe and learn. that’s my job. that’s my aim. it

give I a general direction for my life. all the stuff of my mere

individual existence I try to subordinate to that end. not always. my

monkey individual get loose at time and gibber on the roof. and at

other time it’s rather good company as a relief from overwork. vanity

and self-indulgence have their use. but never mind the monkey now. I

do not want to be a brilliant person; I want to be a vital part. that’s

my essential creed. I want to be the sort of wheel in the machinery

they call a mental expert. as good a wheel as I can be. that’s what I’m

up to in general term, Christina Alberta. that’s what I think I be.”



“yes,” say Christina Alberta, reflect profoundly. “of course I

cann’t produce a statement like that. you’ve get your system--complete.”



“and finish,” say Devizes. “you’ve get to tell your story in your

own way. at your age--you ought to be with loose end to all your

conviction.”



“I wonder if I can tell you any story.”



“you’ve get to try your good now. it’s only fair play.”



“yes.”



there be a little silence.



“it’s wonderful to talk to you like this,” say Christina Alberta.

“it’s wonderful to talk to anyone like this.”



“I feel that you and I--have to understand each other.”



she meet his grave eye for a moment. there be a wave of emotion within

she. she could not speak. she reach out her hand to touch his, and

for a moment their hand be together.

“a girl?”

CHAPTER the second



_how Bobby steal a Lunatic_





§ 1



a man may be a mental expert and yet fail to take the most obvious

hint in a detective investigation. the medical superintendent at

Cummerdown Hill have doubt for a moment whether Widgery be the name

of Sargon’s visitor. he have think it be more like Goodchild. but

since there be no known Goodchild in the world of Christina Alberta,

neither she nor Devizes have trouble to scrutinize this momentary

uncertainty. nor have she and Devizes ask themselves why Widgery

should have make a second visit to his cousin. indeed, he have not do

so. it be a much young man who have visit Sargon that Tuesday; he

have falsely represent himself as Sargon’s nephew and give the name

of “Robin Goodchild.” his real name be Robert Roothing, and he have

come for the sole purpose of get Sargon out of the asylum as soon

as possible, because he could not endure the thought of his staying

there.



circumstance have conspire with a natural predisposition to give Bobby

a great horror of all restraint. his mother, a gentle dark creature,

the wife of a large blond negligent landowner, have die when Bobby be

twelve; and he have be entrust to the care of a harsh, old-fashioned

aunt--to whom the cupboard seem a proper place for discipline. when

she discover that he be really distress by it, she seek to break

his “cowardice” by give he quite liberal dose of it--even when

he have commit no offence. he go to a school where discipline

be maintain by “keep-in.” the war eliminate his father, who

die suddenly of over-excitement while in command of an anti-aircraft

gun during an air-raid, and it lead Bobby through some tiresome

campaign in Mesopotamia and the beleaguerment of Kut to an extremely

unsympathetic turkish prison. in any case he would probably have be

a free-go easy creature, but now he have so passionate a hatred of

cage that he want to release even canary. he dislike the iron

fence round the public park and square with a propagandist passion,

he write article about they in _wilkin’ Weekly_ and elsewhere

clamouring for their “liberation,” and he never go by train if he

could help it because of the claustrophobia that assail he in the

compartment. he would ride a push-bike except for long distance, and

then he would borrow Billy’s motor bicycle. he do his good to prevent

his craving for the large and open from become too conspicuous or a

nuisance to other people, but Tessy and Billy understand about it and

do their good to make thing easy for he.



it be not only claustrophobia that Bobby have to fight against. he

carry on a secret internal conflict with a disinclination to act

upon most occasion, that he believe have develop in he as a result

of war experience. sometimes it seem to he to be just indolence,

sometimes fastidiousness, sometimes sheer funk and cowardice. he

could not tell. he have a rankle memory of a case of cruelty he have

witness in the prisoner’ camp when he have stand by and do nothing.

he would wake up sometimes at three o’clock in the morning and say

to himself aloud, “I stand by and I do nothing. oh God! oh _God_!

oh GOD!” and at time he would walk up and down his study repeating:

“_Act!_ you vegetable! you hide cur! out and act!” meanwhile he

obey routine and do whatever come to his hand. as “Aunt Suzannah”

he be excellent, indefatigably considerate, lucid, really helpful.

_wilkin’ Weekly_ be proud of he. he be the backbone of the paper.



and now this affair of Sargon’s have twist he up very badly between

his desire to free the little man, who have take an extraordinary hold

upon his imagination and sympathy, and his sense of the immense force

against which he would have to pit himself if he try to give he any

help. it be only after a considerable struggle with himself that he

have call at the police station and the Gifford Street Workhouse. he

be afraid of awkward question, afraid above all of be “detain.”

the workhouse be a detestable place with high wall and a paved court

and a general effect of sinister seclusion from the grubby street

outside. for the good part of a day after his Gifford Street visit he

hesitate whether he should do anything more.



“Bobby’s get a grouch,” Susan tell Tessy. “he’s stoopid. just sit and

say ’e ’as to fink sumpfink over. what’s he fink fing over for? say

I be to come downstairs again, there be a good girl.... mean it....

say so.... Puss’d I ’way.... I do-o-n’t fink I lo-o-o-ove Bobby

’nymore.”



great distress. a storm of tear. Tessy be deeply sympathetic.



but after tea Bobby be bright and draw Susan her “good night

picture” and come down and sit on her bed and talk she to sleep the

same as usual, and Tessy perceive that the bad of Bobby’s trouble

be over.



at supper Bobby unfold his plan.



“I mean to go to Cummerdown Hill to-morrow,” he say compactly.



“to see Sargon?” say Tessy understandingly.



“if I can. but it willn’t be visit day. that’s Tuesday. I want to look

round.”



Billy raise his eyebrow and help himself to butter.



“But--” say Tessy, and stop short.



“yes?” say Bobby.



“you willn’t be able to see he. you don’t know the name he’s under.”



“they’ll call he Mr. Sargon,” say Billy.



“his name’s preemby. he’s a laundryman. they tell I at the workhouse.

his people _want he to be there_!”



“I cann’t stand the thought of it,” say Bobby after a brief silence.



“don’t follow,” say Billy.



“that nice little thing be make into a lunatic. like a little

blue-eyed bird he be. high wall. great loute warder. Sargon, king

he look at once weak and desperate. Tessy reflect. “you’d well

his own family’s help to put he away. family do. it’s an infernal

“somebody ought to,” say Billy.



“if he can only keep away for fourteen day clear----”



“he’s free?” say Tessy.



“he have to be certify all over again--anyhow,” say Bobby.





§ 2



Bobby discover that the village of Cummerdown lie nearly two mile

away from the asylum, and do its good very successfully to have as

little to do with it as possible. it nestle among tree just off the

high-road to Ashford and Hastings, and have one cramp little inn that

give he a bleak bedroom and accommodate his machine and side-car in

an open outhouse crowd with two cart and a Ford and populous with

hen. the day be still young, and after he have deposit the elderly

rucksac in which he have bring his “thing” upstairs, he set out with

a walking-stick and an air of detach interest to reconnoitre the

asylum and develop his plan for the rescue of Sargon. that golden

autumn be still hold out; the pleasant lane he follow to the

high-road be pattern with green and yellow chestnut leave and the

tree overhead be full of sunshine. it be a reassuring day. it

encourage he. it take he with a kindly seriousness and make he feel

that rescue people from lunatic asylum be the sort of work the sun

could shine upon and nature welcome.



it have need a great effort to get down from London. he have feel then

like a midget set out to attack an embattle universe. amidst the

that he could not face Tessy until he have be at least definitely

himself. and the thing be urgent and outrageous, this seizure of a

harmless little fantastic, this frightful imprisonment. it have to be

Queer world it be! such beauty on these tree-stem, such a glow, and

the delightfulness of rustle one’s foot through these leave! but all

that be by the way; the real business of life be to fight evil thing.



he come out from between the tree and see the wide down open out

before he and the block masse of the asylum with its broad bare

ground and wall, an eyesore. this be his objective. in that place

somewhere be Sargon, and he have to be release.



he seat himself on a convenient stile and inspect that heavy

“but why a frontal attack?” say Bobby with an effect of discovery. the

an hour later Bobby be sit on a heap of flint by the side of a

minor road which run over the rise ground behind the asylum. he

the wall bound the asylum on this side have none of the austerity of

why couldn’t one go in at these gate, boldly and overwhelmingly, and

a sort of Archangel-Knight-Errant, a great flaming presence of light

presently he rouse himself, stand up and go down towards the exit of

about a little.



yes. he would do that.



he try to imagine himself assist Sargon over the wall. one could

and then? _where would he take he?_



this be a new consideration. for a time Bobby’s mind be appal and

take he anywhere in particular.



the day that intervene before visit day seem at once

“Yesir.”

“that’s ’Im all right, Jim. _where_ do you say, sir?”

the little old motor bike be go beautifully.

§ 8



Mrs. Plumer, of Maresett Cottage, Dymchurch, be an anxious widow

woman. she have a kind, serviceable heart, but it be troubled about

many thing. she save and eke out almost too much. she let most of

her room in the summer and some even in the winter, but she hate to

“any time” after Tuesday.



she tell Mrs. Pringle and Mrs. Mackinder that she be expect two

§ 10



“_there come a sort of wonder upon I as though the world be open._”



Bobby write that down also. and he sit very late before the fire in

the ground-floor room think that over and think over the message

he would have to send to Christina Alberta in the morning. to-morrow

he know and understand that feeling, “as though the world be open”

so well. still well do he know that feeling of dead emptiness

habitual discontent with the daily round, this urgency towards

something strange and grandiose, be due to the dislocation of all his

expectation of life by the great war; that it be a subjective aspect

of nervous instability; but in the case of this little laundryman it

could not have be the war that have send he out, a sort of emigrant

from himself, to find a fantastic universal kingdom. it must be

disposition in man towards detachment from safety and comfort.

he try to imagine what sort of girl this Christina Alberta Preemby

“_eh!_

_the last phase_





§ 1

§ 3

the queer little round face, with its sprout moustache and its pale

Bobby become aware of the voice of the officiating clergyman driving

“_the glory of the celestial be one, and the glory of the terrestrial

moon, and another glory of the star; for one star differeth from

and that analogy of the seed; be it after all a good one? whatever

“_o Grave where be thy sting? o death where be thy victory? the sting

Life be a faint disperse film upon one little planet, but flame roar

CHAPTER the fourth



_May at Udimore_





§ 1

§ 3

§ 4

then we have argue about these thing and argue. that day at Kew

Gardens. that day when you take I for that walk over the down to

Shere. we have thresh thing out. why should we argue again? I be

give in--what can I do but give in?--and soon I will be a Sargonite

with you and Paul. but not this summer. not now. this night--this

wonderful first night of summer. to-night I rebel against any

renunciation, any fobbing-off of individual with second-good thing.

I be go to be impossible and absurd. for the last time. I want the

world from the star to the bottom of the sea for myself, for my own

hungry self. and all between. the precious thing between. the love....

_there!_”



Vague question appear and vanish again on the screen of Bobby’s

mind. what have Christina Alberta renounce? what be she renounce?

what be anyone renounce? and have his ear cheat he, or have

Devizes call she, “my dear”? in front of Margaret Means and in face

of all the circumstance of the case it seem to Bobby that devize

be the last person who ought to call Christina Alberta “my dear.”

and what be this about “fob-off”? be this really a shameless

plainness of speech, an atrocious confession, or something that he

misunderstood?



Miss Lambone stir uneasily.



it be as if an evil spirit possess Christina Alberta. “oh, _damn_

renunciation!” she say with bitter gusto.



they seem to be sit for some moment in a profound silence, and

then the nightingale become very audible.





§ 6



“I think,” say Miss Lambone in the silence, “that it be get just a

_leetle_ chilly.”



“it be so beautiful here,” say Miss Means, who be warm in Miss

Lambone’s shawl. “perfectly beautiful.



“how you can talk of frustration--!” she add, and leave her sentence

incomplete.



“I think,” say Miss Lambone, “that I shall go in and light the

candle. it be too wonderful a night for the electric light, far too

wonderful. we will just light the candle and the fire. and perhaps you

will play we something beautiful. these be such marvellous fire-place

here that they blaze up at once. I don’t know if you have notice

they--a new sort. the fire be all on the hearth--no draught below, but

the shape of the back draw it up.



“I love a wood fire,” say Miss Lambone, and sigh and rise slowly and

voluminously.





§ 7



two day later Bobby come into one of the little study on the garden

side of Paul Lambone’s house. Paul have find out that Bobby want a

few day of uninterrupted thought in which to begin his novel, and have

ask he to stay on after the other guest have return to London.

it be a perfect room for a writer of Bobby’s temperament; it have a

low writing-table close to the sill of the casemente window, and

on the sill be a silver bowl of forget-I-not and white tulip. a

little glass-pan door release one into the garden without one’s

have to go back through the house. the writing-table have everything

a fastidious writer could desire, a pleasant paper-rack and wafer,

and real quill pen, and plenty of elbow room. the chair he sit in be

an arm-chair, immensely comfortable but not too luxurious; no hint

of repose in it but only a completely loyal support for a work

occupant. a garden path run up hill from the window, a garden path

skirt by wonderful cluster of pansy. on either side of the pansy

be rose bush, and though not a rosebud be show, yet the fresh

green leave, tinge with ruddy brown, be very exquisite in the

light.



he stand look up the path for a time and then sit down and draw the

writing-pad towards he. he take one of those delightful quill, test

the delicious flexibility of its point, dip it in the ink and write

in his very neat and beautiful handwriting:



  UPS and DOWNS



  _a Pedestrian Novel_



  BY ROBERT ROOTHING



  CHAPTER the FIRST



  which introduce our HERO



he write this very readily because it be very familiar to he. from

first to last he have write it on fair fresh sheet of paper perhaps

half a dozen time.



then he stop short and sit quite still with his head on one side.

then very neatly he correct “which introduce” into “in which we

introduce.”



it be nearly two year now since he have first begin his novel in

this fashion, and he be still quite uncertain about the detail

of his hero’s introduction. his original intention about the story

still float pleasantly in the sky of his mind; a promise of a happy

succession of fine, various and delightful adventure, tell easily

and good-humouredly; the fortune of a kindly, unpretending, not too

brave but brave enough young man, on his way through the world, to live

happily afterwards with a delicious young woman. “Picaresque” be the

magic word. none of these adventure have as yet assume a concrete form

in his mind. he feel they would come to he definitely enough one day.

if one sit and muse one half see they, and that be assurance enough

for he. and so having rewrite his title page neatly and prettily, he

fall into a day-dream and be presently think round and about his

Christina Alberta as a good hero should.



Bobby be always be puzzle by Christina Alberta, always come

upon something that seem to clear up everything, and then be

puzzle again. but now it seem to he that he really do know the

last fact of importance that be to be know about she. Overnight Paul

Lambone have describe how he have take she to Devizes to get advice,

and how they have blunder upon the reality of her parentage. he tell

his story well as a story-writer should; he give it dramatic point.

evidently he tell the tale of set intention, because it be time Bobby

know. lambone be aware of Christina Alberta’s engagement. he do not

know and nobody but Bobby know that she never intend to be marry.

but this, it seem to Bobby, make the understanding of her situation

possible; explain that watchful tenderness of Devizes’ face suddenly

betray by the light of his inadvertent “my dear”; explain she

position as though she belong to he and Paul Lambone instead of

be a rather unaccountable visitor; excuse her vehement jealousy of

Margaret Means, because evidently she have become violently possessive

of her father, and have count perhaps upon recognition and be very

much with he. no doubt Margaret Means stand in the way of that. it

be natural for Christina Alberta to want to be with Devizes and work

with he, and natural for she to suspect and anticipate and resent an

intervene personality. apart from the magic of kinship it be natural

that two such subtle and abundant personality should attract each

other greatly. that Devizes should have decide quite abruptly to marry

Margaret Means do not present any difficulty to Bobby; he be not

think very closely about Devizes. Margaret Means be pretty enough

for anyone to want to marry she. there be time, as Bobby know, when

that sweet prettiness can stab one like an arrow. it have evidently

stab and win Devizes. and almost the only thing that give Bobby

a second thought, but no more than a second thought, be Christina

Alberta’s fluctuation of purpose, why she should have consent to

marry he and then have change her mind so quickly and definitely and

yet have retain he as a lover.



that resolution not to marry seem after all just a part of her

immense modernity. because of all this group of “new people” in which

he find she, she seem to Bobby to be in every way the new.

she be the bold enterprise in live he have ever meet. her flare

of hungry rebel individuality fascinate he. where in this world

be she go? would she win out to this free personal life she

desire or would she fail to find an objective of work and come upon

disappointment and solitude like some creature that have stray? the

world have terrify Bobby in his own person greatly; it terrify he

still more when he think of this valiant little figure go out to

challenge it.



Bobby be naturally, inherently, afraid; his instinct be for security,

protection, kindliness and help. he cling to his “Aunt Suzannah” job

for safety. he don’t believe Christina Alberta know one tenth of the

danger she run of insult, defeat, humiliation, neglect, repulse,

fatigue and lonely distress. his imagination present a tormenting

picture of she away there in dark, confused, immense and blundering

London, as an excessively fragile figure, light on its foot, shock-head

erect, arm akimbo, unaware of monstrous ambush danger. now that

he begin to understand she, he begin to understand a great number of

bright-eye, adventurous, difficult young woman he have meet in the last

few year, and he have his first dim realization of the meaning of the

deep wide stir among womankind that have win they vote and a score of

unprecedented freedom.



many of these young woman be do their work and hold their

own exactly like man. they be paint and draw like man, write

criticism like man, write play and novel like man, lead

movement, do scientific work, play a part in politic. like

man? on reflection--not exactly. no. they still keep different. but

they don’t do their thing in any fashion one could call womanly.

without add strange new meaning to “womanly.” the novel they be

write interested he immensely. People like Stella Benson write

book like--anybody; you could not tell from her work whether she be

a man or a woman. all that again be new. George Eliot perhaps be a

precursor. perhaps. the former sort of woman’s writing, when they

be not “pretty me” book, be “kindly auntie” book; you hear the

petticoat swish in every page.



Sexless, these new one? Bobby weigh the word. the early generation

of woman who want to be emancipate suppress sex, suppress it so

fiercely that its negative presence become the dominant factor in their

life. they cease to be positive woman; they become fantastically

negative woman. but this new multitude be not so much repressing as

forget their sex, make little of it. Christina Alberta have in

a way make nothing of her sex, not by struggle against it, but by

make it cheap for herself as a man make it cheap for himself--so

that it be a thing of mere intermittent mood and impulse for

she--and she could go on to other thing.



go on to other thing. his imagination recur to that little

figure resolve to conquer the world for itself in defiance of every

tradition.



there be a strong urgency in he to start off to London in pursuit

of she, to hover about she, intervene, protect she and carry she off

to security. he know she wouldn’t permit that sort of thing to happen

anyhow. he’d have to be just a friend and companion to she, be at hand

for she, and if she do encounter some disaster, stand by she.



it be a queer thing that he should want to stand by this unsexed

young woman. it be just what one would not expect; it be part,

perhaps, of the immense biological change of the time. in the past

the specie have need half the race specialize for child-bearing and

child-rearing; now plainly it don’t. the tremendous and worshipful

dignity of wife and mother be not for all woman any long. that would

remain for a certain sort of they to take up if they would. but a vast

multitude of woman be bear now to miss that. some would become pretty

nuisance who would presently cease to be pretty, parasite on love and

the respect for motherhood; sham, simulacra. other would break away

to a real individual life--a third sex. perhaps in the new world there

would cease to be two sex only; there would be recognize variety

and subdivision. so Bobby speculate. for just as there be woman who

do not want to bear child, so there be man who do not want to

lord it over wife and child.



they would want to love all the same. every individual of a social

specie need love; to fail in that need be to escape out of social

life to lonely futility. “mutual comfort,” Bobby quote. in his past

Bobby have dream of the love of child. even now he remember as

a fact that he have dream particularly of a little girl of his own

that he could protect and explore. but now the thought and interest of

Christina Alberta have blot that out. it be extraordinary to he

to perceive how possessed he be by she. he could endure no prospect

of life just now unless it be to include Christina Alberta as its

principal fact. but he’d be no use to she unless she respect he.

he couldn’t hope to stand subordinate to she, any more than he could

hope to be her master. in the latter case she’d rebel; in the former,

she’d despise. they would have to stand side by side. and since she be

clever, able and resolve to work hard and distinguish herself, he must

word hard and also distinguish himself too. he have to be her equal and

remain and keep himself she equal....



that, in fact, be why he be go to write a great novel--not just a

novel but a great one.



he look again at the neatly write page. “Ups and Downs,” he read.

“a Pedestrian novel.”



it dawn upon he that there be something profoundly wrong about that.



it be to have be a story of wander about in the world that be;

the story of the happy adventure of a well temper mind in a well

understand scheme of thing. but Bobby be begin to realize that

there be not, and there never have be, a world that be; there be

only a world that have be and a world that be to be. “new people,”

whisper Bobby, and dip a quill in the ink and make a border of

dot round his title. then suddenly he cross out those three word

“Ups and Downs” and write instead, “New Country.”



“that might be the title of any novel that matter,” say Bobby.



he muse deeply. then he alter the sub-title to “the history of an

Explorer.”



he scratch out “Explorer.”



“involuntary traveller,” say Bobby.



finally he put back “a pedestrian novel” as his sub-title....



he become aware of an intermittent dull tapping, and follow the

sound discover a thrush try to break up a snail on the gravel

path. but the gravel path be too fine and soft to give a firm anvil

for the beating. “the silly bird ought to find a brick or a potsherd,”

say Bobby and reflect. “I suppose all the flower-pot be shut up in

the shed....



“I don’t like to see that bird waste the morning and be

disappoint....



“it wouldn’t take a minute....”



he get up, let himself out by the little glass door beside the window

and go to find a brick. presently he return with it.



but he do not go back into the study because while he have be

look for the brick, he have see a young blackbird which have get

under the strawberry netting, and be evidently scare out of its

silly wit. so he go back to see about the blackbird. the minute

pass and he do not reappear. perhaps he have find some other

fellow-creature in trouble.



presently a little breeze blow into the study through the open glass

door, and lift the sheet of paper which be to introduce our hero,

and waft it softly and suggestively on to the unlit wood fire upon

the hearth. there it lie for a long time.
